O, sons, because I have completed this book for you, which is the last of the science of medicine, by the fulfillment of it, its end is achieved in it, and by its expositions and by its declarations, it is my view that you complete this for you with this treatise, which is a part of the operation with my, so it is surgery.
Because the operation with my hand is laid low in our region and in our time completely private, while many perils the science of it, and its vestiges are held hostage; and there remain of it only some small descriptions in the books of the Ancients, which have been altered and become subject to those errors and confusion because their clauses are understood and the force of it and the art are elongated.
And it is seen to me that you bring to life that with the arrangement of this treatise, in accordance with the way of exposition and declaration and abbreviation; and for that it comes with the forms of the tools of cautery and of the other instruments of the work, as it is for the addition of the declaration, and for the preparation of it, which cause is necessary.
But the cause for which no good artificer with his hand is found in our time, is that the art of medicine is long, and that it requires the actor to be previously exercised in the science of anatomy, which recounts the Gualia, because it knows the joints of the members and the forms of those, and the junction and separation of them; and it has the knowledge of the bones, of the nerves and of the cartilages, and the number of those, and the emergence of them; and of the pulsating veins and of the quiet ones, and of the places of the emission of them.
And for that reason, Hippocrates says that with a name is nothing, but in the work little, and most of all in the art of the hand.
But we have already said of that in the introduction of this book; for whoever is not acquainted with what we have said of anatomy, is not evacuated that he will not fall into error for which many men die, as I have seen too many of those, some are formed in this science and throw themselves from that science and their experience.
For I saw a foolish and inexperienced physician who had incised an abscess scrophilos in the neck of a woman, and incised some arteries of the neck so that great blood flowed, and the woman died between her hands.
And I saw another physician who prescribed that a stone from a man who had processed in his state be swallowed, and the stone was large, and it came to him and extracted that stone with a piece of the bladder, for that man on the third day died.
And I had already appealed to extract that, and I saw that because of the size of the stone and the disposition of the patient, I knew above him that he would die.
And I saw another physician who took a stipend from a duke of our land for surgery, and he came to a black crassat who was broken in his hip; and prepared the physician with his ignorance, extracted the fracture, over the wound, with splints and splints with strong structure, and did not leave the wound to breathe.
And then he set it according to his desires, and then he ligated it, and then he left it for some days, and commanded that we untie the ligament; and it stood thus, that the hip and the foot became infected, and it came to ruin.
And I was called to him, and I appeared to untie the ligament, and achieved tranquility and thus, he was free of his pains.
But however, the corruption had already been confirmed in the limb, so that I could not restrain it, and it did not cease to wander the corruption in the limb until the patient perished.
And I saw another physician who perforated a cancerous abscess, and it became ulcerated after some days, until the malice of it was magnified to its lord.
The cause of which is that the cautery is pure of melancholic humor, so that it does not come to it with iron at all, but that it be in a member that suffers that it be held captive by all.
And therefore, sons, it is necessary for you that the operation with my hand be devised in two divisions: that is, in operation that is associated with health, and in work with which there is peril in the major dispositions.
And I have already exerted this in all places of this book in which I come across operation in which there is error and fear; for it is necessary for you that you guard this and leave that, so that the foolish do not come to speak and to dishonor you.
And therefore, take your arms with diligence and with the protection of God, and your patients with facility and with firmness.
And use the best way by the grace of God, pursuing health and praiseworthy success, and leave the diseases terrible of difficult healing, and hold your arms from that which makes you fear, so that hesitation in your faith and in your name does not escape you: for it is remaining to your glory, and more so in the world and in the end to your sanctitates.
Gualia already said in some of his monitions: “Do not treat bad diseases, so that bad physicians are not called.”
And I have now divided this book according to three chapters.
Begins the division of the book, and the first of the first part. The first chapter ordered of the head within feet, in the forms of the instruments and in the tools of the cauteries, and of that which is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, of suction and perforation, and of cupping glasses, and of leeches, and of the extraction of arrows and of things similar to those, all chaptered and ordered of the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, of restoration and dislocation, and of the cure of sprains and of things similar to those; chapter ordered of the head within feet, and of the forms of the instruments.
I say that you tell the quality of the judgments of that cautery and of its consequences, and in what completion it should be prohibited cautery.
Therefore I say that the sermon of the quality of the judgment of cautery and of its consequences is a long and prolix sermon, and subtle by science, and secret hidden; and already of it have spoken a great multitude of wise men, and of it they have been disagreed, and to me suffices a little of the sermons of theirs, fearing prolongation.
And therefore I say that cautery compares to all complections universally which is in matter or its matter, except two complections, which are complections caudas its matter.
In the cauda and dry complection in matter they disagreed; for some said that cautery is beneficial to that, and the others said the contrary of that, that cautery is not convenient in illness which is by quality and by dryness, for the nature of fire is quality and dryness, and inconvenient is that illness cauda and dry with that which is caut and dry be healed.
And they said those who say the contrary of that, that by cautery is made benefit in all cauda and dry illness that comes in the bodies of men, for when you compare the body of man and its humidities to the complexion of fire, you find the body of man cold.
And I say for the sermon of that, that experience this to me some times has discovered.
But however, it does not come upon this that which already is explored and exercised in the chapter of cautery by previous exercise, and has known the diversity of the complexion of men and the disposition of the illnesses in their own bodies, and the causes of those, and their accidents, and the space of time of those.
But in the other complections, it is not upon you fear, and mostly in the cold and humid illnesses, for already all the physicians have agreed upon those, and do not disagree in the judgment with cautery in those.
And know, sons, that of the secret of cure by cautery with fire and of the goodness of it, it is because the fire is simple, the action of which does not reach beyond the member which is cauterized and does not reach the other member attached to it except with little consequence.
But the action of burning medicine reaches to that in the members which are distant from it, and greatly makes them come in the member illness of difficult healing and greatly difficult.
But the fire, for its sublimity and of its substance goodness, does not do that except that it is superfluous, and already it is to us known by experiment and by length of service of study in the truth of the causes; and for that I am excused from the length of the sermon, and if it were not that it is not read in my book in this pause to you secret hidden with the fire, and the quality of its operation in the bodies and of its expulsion by which it expels the illnesses, with a philosophical sermon demonstrative, which would be acute in your minds.
And know, sons, that they disagree of the time in which it is convenient to do cautery and pause the best time of spring.
And I say that cautery is convenient in all times, because so that the consequence for the time accident is submerged in the benefit of that which with the cautery is thrown, and mostly if the cautery is for pains or of necessary pains, strong and fistulous, which do not suffer tardiness because it is feared of those that come malice, that is greater than little consequence entering for the time.
And thus do not catch in your minds, oh, sons, that this is not esteemed but the vulgar people and the foolish physicians, so it is that to that illness which is healed by cautery is not said all times, and they pause that consequence.
And the cause is not at all so as they think about it, because cautery is not but in place of medicine which alters the complexion and dries the humidities which are cause for that pains come.
But however, cautery superfluous over the medicine for the desire of its validity and virtue of its operation and greatness of its lord.
And it is possible that its illness returns in some hour of the time, according to the complexion of the patient and the location of its illness, and the virtue of it, and that which is prepared in the body of it of the aggregation of the superfluities in it, and that which is reached to it in its acquisition in victuals and things similar to those causes; otherwise that the illness in which cautery is administered is a subtle illness in a member of few superfluities and humidities, as well as cautery of tooth for pain and things similar to that, it is possible that in it it does not return that pain, but it is made in little.
And more common speech is that cautery is the last remedy of medicine, and it is right speech, not that I understand in it, because I believe that there is no cure that benefits with medicine or with any other cause except cautery.
And the cause is in contrast to that; and the understanding that cautery is the last remedy of medicine is only when they administer the methods of cure in some illnesses, and those medicines do not confer, and then at the end of the cause one administers cautery, and it benefits to that. And therefore, that cautery is the last remedy of medicine, not according to the understanding that the common people and many foolish doctors understand.
And the ancients said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they did not say this except for the equality of gold and the sublimity of its substance.
And they said that the place of cautery does not make a scar, as it is not absolutely true, because I have already seen that and found that it does not do so except in some bodies, and cautery with that is good and better than with iron as they said; however, when you do cautery with fire with gold, it is not declared to you, because it is ignited according to the quantity that you want by the heat of the gold, and that it prepares to receive heat; and if you adjust it on fire, the gold melts by the fire, and in the fire some sheets so that the craftsman catches for the occupation of that.
And for that reason, cautery with iron should be done more lightly and more carefully to the rectitude of operation.
And I have already ordered this chapter on cautery according to chapters, and I have ordered those of the head within the feet, so that later the one demanding that wants from it.
The first chapter.
This cauterization confers on the humidity and coldness over the nerves of which are two causes, so it is the cause of soda and multitude of rheumatic flux, called corissa, from the head to the parts of the ears and of the ears flowing, and multitude of sleep, and of toothache and of the goiter, and universally of all illness that comes from coldness, as well as paralytically, soda and apoplexy, and similar in those illnesses.
The form of this cauterization is this, that first you command the patient to be evacuated with laxative medicine the head modifying, for three or for four nights, according to that you require the virtue of the illness and the age of him and his custom.
And then command him to shave his head, and then make that seat between your hands on a low bench and that he place his hands on his feet.
And then place the root of your palm near the nose between his heels. But however, as your saying of the middle place has arrived, sign that place with a ten, and then ignite the cautery olive of which this is the form:
And then place that on the place signed with a ten, in the manner that it descends, with which your hand is constricted little by little, and you revolve that.
And then lift your hand slightly and look at the place, and if you see already of the bone being uncovered some quantity of the head, as well as the quantity of a grain of ears, then raise your hand, and if not return your hand with that iron, with another of that, if it is cooled, until it is seen of the bone that I have told you.
And then take a little salt and dissolve that on the place and leave that for three days.
And then place on it a cloth soaked in oil and leave that on it until the scar of the fire is destroyed. And then cure the place with tetrafarmacon ointment until it is healed.
And they already said that when the plague remains open, so much better it gets the scar, because it is better and more helpful.
And they said some of them that it should be cauterized the spine into the bone, and that the cautery should be held until some part of the whiteness of the bone is burned.
And then scrape that which is burned of the bone, and then it should be cured.
And they said others that with the cautery it should reach the rear, until it presses into the bone forcefully until the bone breaks, and this is the form of alphecati small: And they estimate that from that place the vapors of the head expire.
And it should be kept open for a long time, and then it should be cured until it is consolidated.
But to me, these two kinds of cautery have not been seen, except in some men according to their way of fear; and leave that, of me, it is better for health, when it is; because the head is opened by the solution of continuity of it as well as we have testified in the other members, and most of all when the head of the patient is of a cold nature.
And the species of cautery first is more saved and better of me; and of that use and with that work with health.
Second chapter.
Of the cautery of the head.
When chronic and long-lasting pain occurs throughout the head and it is prolonged in the patient, and use of herbs, potions, purgatives, plasters, poultices, compresses, and other remedies, and mostly if it has already been cauterized with a cautery which they recount and does not benefit him in any of those causes, then consider that if the head of the patient is of strong creation in its nature; and is not frail and finds a very great burning, then cauterize him with another cautery besides that one a little. And then cauterize that on the horn of his head with a cautery while the code is parted. Incise the code and uncover the amount of it which we have recounted and cauterize that with a cautery in the back part of the head in the place which is called vas, — and that is the place in which the cupping glass is placed. And alleviate your hand in that, and do not uncover the bone, because the patient by that finds much great pain different from the pain of all the other cauteries of the head.
And you say this cautery in its place, because whoever cauterizes with the cautery with which the horns of the head and the nape are cauterized is more subtle cautery than that with which the middle part of the head is cauterized; and this is the form of it.
Chapter Three.
Of cauterization of  a non-ancient hernia.
When pain in the head is felt, or pain extends into the eye, it should be evacuated with medicines that modify the head, and the other cure should be administered, which I have said in the divisions of the diseases; and if nothing helps, then cauterization in it is in two ways: either cauterization with acute medicine that burns, or with iron.
But cauterization with burning medicine is that you take a handful of alum, and you scratch that and separate the extremities of it from both ends. And then incise the place of the pain in the temple with a lancet wide while it is in it made a place after the code, while it is hidden.
And then strengthen over it with a stopper for descent constriction, and leave that for the space of three hours; and then untie the and pull the alum, and leave the ulcer for two or three days, and then place over it a cloth submerged in broth while the place makes a crust.
And then cure that with an enema while it is healing.
And if you want, make this with some burning medicines which I have written in the two treatises of burning medicines.
But cauterization with iron according to this manner: ignite a cautery of which this is the form:
and it is called a clove cautery, because the head of it is as shaped like a clove in which there is some curvature, and in the middle part of it is a small eminence.
And then place over the place of the pain, and hold your hand — so that the artery which is below is not burned — and revolve the iron little by little with speed.
And if the amount of it that is burned by the sharpness of the bone is as much as most of it, and remove your hand so that the artery which is below is not burned, so that no flow of blood comes.
And then imbibe cloth in salt water and place that over the place, and leave that for three days; and then place cloth ground with broth, and then cure with an enema while it is healing.
And if you want, cauterize with the extremity of the knife-like cautery, and guard yourself from incision of the artery in this hernia properly non-ancient.
Chapter Four.
Of cauterization of an ancient hernia.
When you cure a hernia with what we have said of the previous cure, and what we have said in the divisions of the diseases, and if it does not help the cure, and sometimes because of the illness that does not reach it what we have said of the first cauterization with medicine or with fire, then you must ignite the knife-like cautery while it is white-hot, because it signifies over the place of the pain with a line of length of which most of it is said, or where that is; and hold your hand for a moment and strengthen that while you incise the artery and reach into the bone.
However, it is necessary for you to guard against the continuation of the jawbone, which is bad for chewing, and that the lacert is not burned and the nerve which feels that, and that it does not become a spasm.
And if it is about the custody and caution of the flow of blood from the artery that is incised, because in the incision of it there is fear, and especially to him who ignores what is done and is not studious and experienced; and leave the operation in it at the beginning.
And I will come with remembrance of the regimen of blood flow accident of the artery according to its manner, in its place in this book.
And if you see of this illness that this cauterization does not suffice and you see the body of the patient tolerable, cauterize that in the middle part of the head as we have recounted, and cure the ulcer while it is healed.
And if you are, administer the cautery which we have said in the chapter of the cauterization of the artery, with a cautery having two handles, and this cauterization is better and more convenient.
Chapter V.
Of cauterization of ear pains.
When in the ear there is pain due to heat and it is cured with laxative causes and with the other cure which I have said in the division, and the pain is not removed, then ignite the cautery, and this is the one which has the name point, of which this is the form:
And when pointed, ignite it, and it is led around the entire ear as a wheel is rolled, or in the circuit of whichever part if the pain is in whichever. Lengthen it with the cautery of the root of the ear little by little as if you were sensing the place with a probe.
And if the cautery of ten points in the whole ear, and that one, and then cure the places while they are healing.
Chapter VI.
Of cauterization of tumors.
Tumors which are cured and with cauterization are nothing but of the kinds which come from phlegm, according to what we have said in the divisions of diseases.
Avoid cauterization of the kind that comes from dryness or spasm of the nerve.
When you cure that kind of tumor with herbs and purgatives and gargles, and if you do not find comfort in your cure, then when you cauterize the malady with three cauteries: the first you must at the root of the ear, the second a little from its temple, and the third you must at the junction of the two lips.
And pause your cautery for the opposite of the diseased part, because softening does not come except in the part that is healthy.
And the form of cauterization is that you cauterize that with the cautery in the right of the extremity of the ear, more sovereign a little over the horn of the head, and in the temple another, the length of which be according to the length of the finger, and pause your hand with the cautery until the quantity of the majority of the species of the codena is burned.
And this is the form of the cautery, and it is a species of the cutlery, the form of which is shown opposite. But however more subtle is that one a little as if you were seeing; and when the handle in its place of superfluous thickness a little.
And then cure the place with that which is oppositely processed until it is healed.
Chapter VII.
Of cauterization of old apoplexy.
When apoplexy is ancient and you cure that with what we have said and you do not find comfort in your medication and the malady has no fever, then cauterize that with four cauteries: on top of all the horns of its head; and with cautery and the middle location of the head, as we have said; and with cautery in the cheekbone. And the manner of the cautery is made according to what has been said.
And already it is cauterized above the mouth of the stomach; and it is more posterior; and then it should be cured with what you process.
Chapter VIII.
Of cauterization of forgetfulness caused by flame.
When those who are given to the patient at the beginning large herbs and pills the serving modifying.
And then shave the head of the entire patient and place on the cheekbone of him a poultice of cetre described in the treatise of poultices.
And pause that some times, for that is the manner of cauterization. And do that according to that same order which I have already said.
And therefore if by those he is healed well; and if not, then do to him three cauteries in the rear part of his head, which are descending from the most sovereign part of the head into the part more below the neck. And pause between each cautery the thickness of the finger, and then cure the cautery with what is processed.
But if you add to it and the malady is that tolerant, then do to him cauterize in the middle location; and if you see greater addition, then cauterize that on the two horns.
Then cure that until it is healed; and let the cautery be olive according to the form previously given.
Chapter IX.
Of cauterization of paralysis and of softening of the whole body.
When you process in modification of the head with herbs and with what we have said; and then shave the head of the patient, and then cauterize that with cautery in the middle location of the head, and with cautery on all the horns of the head, and with cautery on the rear part of him, and with three cauteries on the vertebrae of the neck.
And if it is necessary in the malady of softening of the body of too much that and the maladies tolerant and the malady is strong, then cauterize that with four cauteries on the vertebrae of the loins, and certainly with cautery until it is burned from the codena of him, and raise your hand. And then care for that within that is healed, and this is olive oil cautery.
Chapter X.
Of cauterization of epilepsy.
An epileptic is not cauterized, but that epilepsy is due to phlegm.
And therefore, if one modifies the brain of him at the beginning with years and with the other cure which we have said in the division when the patient is great and is tolerating the excess of medicines.
And if it is a child who does not suffer the medicines, then administer guarguardians and mastiguators to the brain, before that for a few days, with the blessing of the victuals.
And then scrape the head of the patient, and then cauterize it with a cautery in the middle location of the head according to the previous manner, and with another cautery in the nape of his neck, and on each horn of his head cauterize. And if the patient is strong and is tolerating, then cauterize it with cauteries which I have said, having paralytically and softening of the body over the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the lower back.
And be olive cautery according to what is preceded.
But if the patient is a child, then pause subtle cautery according to this manner.
Chapter XI.
Of cauterization of melancholy.
When the causes of melancholy are humidities corrupted and thick phlegm, then cauterize it with the cauteries which we have said in the having paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholy is superfluity declining to melancholy, and the body of the patient is humid, then give him to drink water that purifies the brain of him according to what has preceded in the division.
And then scrape the head of the patient, and then make a decent tourniquet on him over the circle, and then place that in the middle location of the head.
And the patient be sentient, and be held from every part.
And then pause liniment; and it is more worthy of border antic of sheep. And warm that over the fire for moderate heating by measure, which the said suffers when it is most in him. And then pour that in the middle location of the head of him in the circle, and let him inside that it be cooled.
And do this water to the patient every week once, with the other regimen of him well inside that it is healed; or if you will, cauterize that doing small points according to the most of him, unless that you have the hand with a cautery so ordered: this is a special cautery which wets the brain with equality.
And then pause that coated in wool or in grease of guanine.
Chapter XII.
Of cauterization of water descending in the heel.
When to you is declared the beginning of water in the heel by the signs which I have said in the division, then begin and give to the patient to drink water that purifies the head of him, and guard that from all humidities.
And do that to soak in a bath in degue for some days.
And then command him that he scrape his head, and cauterize that with a cautery in the middle location of the head; and then cauterize that with two cauteries on two temples if it is the beginning of the ailment of water in two heels, or of one side if it is the beginning of the water in one heel.
Incise with cautery all the veins and arteries which are left the code; and be the cauteries in that length in the latitude of the temples.
And guard yourself from fluxes of blood when it is cauterized; and if you see from him fluxes of blood, stop it with some medicine, which is to you possible.
And you will come with wisdom which is in the extraction of arteries and with the abscess of those and caution of fluxes of blood.
And sometimes it is cauterized in the neck below two bones with two cauteries deliberately produced.
Chapter XIII.
Of cauterization of old tears.
When tears of the heels are old, suppurating and through the veins and arteries which are in the manifest location of the head outside and it is verified that this is superfluity of cold phlegm, then cauterize that with the cautery which we have recounted in the chapter of the beginning of water causing in the heels, and with a cautery in the middle location of the head and with two cauteries in the neck below two temples, and with two cauteries in the neck below two bones. Chapter XIV.
Of cauterization of nasal stench.

Chapter .xvi.
Of cautery of the eyelid of the wheel when the hairs are converted to the parts of it and prick the wheel.

Chapter XVII.
Of cauterization of fistula that is in the lacrimal of the heel.
When you cure a fistula with what we have said in the divisions of diseases and do not confer your cure, then it is necessary that you cauterize it according to this manner: command the patient to place his head on your knee, and hold his head some minister between your hands in such a manner that it is not moved nor agitated with his head.
And then place a cloth soaked in oil or in a poultice of ptilli on the heel of him.
And then ignite the cautery of which this is the form:
which is concave, as is the shape of a cane or a vulture's feather, from one extremity of which the cautery is made.
And if you wish, it may be penetrating into the other extremity. And if you wish, it may be shaped as a tentacle.
But however, the one that is concave is better for your work.
And then press the fistula if it is open, and draw from it blood and dry it. And if it is not open, then open it and extract the pus from it.
And then place on it the cautery which is well ignited, and hold it with your hand a little from the cautery of the heel to the part of the nose, so that it does not burn your hand or the patient be agitated, and then place the cautery in the grains of the heel and corrupt it.
And therefore if at the beginning of your cautery it penetrates into the bone, it is good; and if not, then iterate the cautery once or twice, if it were necessary; and leave it so for three days, and then place on it a cloth with border and enclose it with an astringent enema until it is healed.
But if after eleven days it is not healed, then place on it an acute corrosive medicine until the bone is uncovered and scraped according to what you have ordained in your chapter.
And there is another way of cauterizing a fistula which some first said:  enter into the place of the fistula and cut it open, and then place in that fissure a fine thread of which this is the form: And put into it the quantity of one ounce of melted lead; and hold your hand with a fine retention, and do not move the patient so that the melted lead does not reach the heel of him; and when you place on the heel of the patient a cloth soaked in oil or in water, because the lead will burn the place of the fistula and heal it by a miraculous healing.
And if the fistula is healed with what we have said of the cauterization and the cure, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary to administer a traction from the nose and reduce the fistula to the core of the nose according to what will come in its place properly.

Chapter XIX.
Of cauterization of a fistula in the mouth.
When in the root of the uvula or in the palate or in the roots of the teeth there should be an abscess and then it breaks, and then it is antiquated, and pus flows and a fistula is made, and then you cure it and your cure does not profit, then it is necessary that you ignite the cautery according to the quantity that it is received in the fistula, and then put that ignited in the tract of the fistula, and hold your hand until it reaches the bottom of it, and at the back of it do so once or twice; and then cure that with the water that we have said of the cure until it is healed.

Chapter XX.
Of cauterization of the teeth and of softening of the angivas.
When the gums are softened by humidity and the teeth are bad, and you cure those with medicines and do not confer, then place the head of the patient on your knee, and then ignite the cautery the form of which will come shortly, so that you place the point on the tooth and introduce into it the ignited cautery quickly, and hold your hand a little so that the patient feels the heat of the fire at the root of the tooth being reached.
And then take your hand away, and then iterate the cautery several times according to what you wish.
And then the patient should rinse their mouth with salt water and keep it there for an hour, and then spit it out, because the bad tooth is stopped, the inflamed gums are softened and the corrupted moisture is dried.
Chapter XXI.
Of cauterizing tooth pain.
When tooth pain is due to cold, or there is a worm in it, or it does not respond to treatment with medicine, then cauterization is done in that tooth in two ways: cauterization with a cow's horn, or cauterization with fire.
Cauterization with horn is that you take the horn of a cow and heat it in a blacksmith's forge, and then take a handle and wrap it over the tip of the handle, and then immerse that in the heated horn, and place it on the painful tooth firmly until it is seared.
And then repeat that several times until the virtue of the fire reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, submerge wool or a stick in the heated horn below, and place it on the painful tooth, and place a red-hot iron on top of it until the iron reaches the tip of the cavity of the tooth.
But however, cauterization with fire is that you wait for the tip of the iron or of the cautery to be heated, and that it has some thickness in it and the sharpness of the fire does not reach the mouth of the patient.
And then ignite the cautery, the shape of which will come, and place it on the tooth and hold your hand over it until the cautery is seared; and do this several times because the pain will be reduced either on that day or the next.
And so that by the impression of that cautery the patient rinses his mouth with good broth and keeps it there for an hour and then spits it out; and this is the shape of the cautery.
And cauterize with whichever extremity you wish according to what is possible for you.
And this is the shape of the cannula:
Chapter XXII.

And if the abscess is small, then place the cautery according to the quantity of the abscess, and then leave it for three days and place a stick soaked in broth on top of it until that which the fire has burned is healed.
And then cure it with gargles and poultices and bandages until it is healed.

Of the cure of alconati.
This disease which is called alconati is nothing else but the one which is adjusted in the wound resembling a descending water and it is not that one.
But the manner of working in it is that you make the patient sit on the saddle arranged and upright, and then take his head in your two hands and pull it so that you see the healing coming to the lower parts, and then let it dry and not descend, and let the light be, and the patient sees the causes as he sees.
And if it does not descend below, you know that it is water.
But if the descent is not prepared to the healing with the water that we have said, then make the patient sit between your hands and then take a subtle spatula and put it on that over the panniculus, carefully removing a little of the continuation of the conjunctiva with the cornea in the crown (in the place where it is void), until the healing is removed.
Therefore, when it is removed, then you distill into the wound water cauda in which you have mixed honey or water in which you have already boiled fennel and honey.
And then cure it with the other causes until it is healed.
Chapter XXII.
Of the cure of the descending water of the wound.
We have already said the species of water in the division and which one you should pierce with a serum, declaring, then that according to the health.
Then, you must make the patient sit between your hands on a chair and tie his wound tightly.
And then place your left hand near the eyelid of his wound if the wound where the water is is the left; or with your right hand, if the wound is the right.
And then with your right hand take an almond, if the wound is left; or with your left hand, if the wound is right.
And then place the tip of the almond near the crown for the thickness of the ray in the album of the wound from the side of the lacrimal gland, and then press the almond with virtue and roll your hand with that.
And then penetrate into the album of the wound and feel that the almond has already arrived at the void cause.
And let the measure of the path of the almond to the bottom be the measure of the space which is from the pupil to the end of the blackness, and it is the crown of the wound; because you will see with that pupil the vision of the wound through the clarity of the tunic cornea.
And then place the almond into the place where the water is, and then you throw it down once, then another, and therefore if the water descends soon, then wet the patient's wound with water over which he looks soon, and almond in his wound.
And then rest for a while; therefore if the water has descended, then you rest that other time until the almond is thrown.
And therefore when it is dried and does not descend, then you extract the almond with ease, and roll with that your hand little by little, and then dissolve in the water a little of clear salt of gemma and with that wash the wound; and then place outside the wound cotton or stopper or wool soaked in rose oil and egg white and lick the wound with water.
But in those that are of our time, place on that comi trissat with egg white.
And if you do not obey your almond to the entrance of the wound because of its hardness — because it is some of the men whose wound is hard very much — then you must take the spatula which is called alberid of which this is the shape:
And therefore pierce with that that juncture, and do not end your hand in the trace, and it is not if you do not make way for the almond place subtle.
And then introduce the almond according to what we have said.
And you must complete the work, prepare the patient to sleep comfortably in a dark room, where he sleeps on his back, and he is prohibited from all movement.
And rest in nature for him so that his nature is purified, and do not turn his head to the right or left at all.
And bind him according to his disposition on the third day.
And then unbind him in that dark room and prohibit the view of the cold, and make him see some causes, and then return the bandage on the seventh day.
And do not cover that you do this at the hour of cures or as soon as the infusion. You must however alienate this so that the water flows gently and with great and vehement regard.
But if an abscess occurs, then you must unbind the wound before the seventh day, and rectify it with water that causes the abscess to subside.
Then you should cover his vision and place a cloth over his face through which his breathing is regulated, leaving him for several days in the dark room.
And then he should be removed from that room gradually and converses about his causes.
And know that in alchemy the disciple is not excused from vision sometimes, and then you should be bold about the operation.
And now we have reached some of those who are in Artach, and some say that they make fire in the Ayrac magdaam perforated with which the water is sucked.
But however, I have not seen any in our land who has perfected water, nor have I read about it in some of the books of the ancients; and it is possible that this is novel.
And these are the forms of the species of magdaan so that it may be on those who ignore those:
And they are made of brass or iron properly, and their extremities are with this triangular subtlety, the acute extremity as such is the form.
Chapter XXIV.
Of the cure of flesh nothing in the nose.
When in the nose are born diverse carns adjusted of which some cause is assembled like a scorpion of moth weight, or a polyp weight; and this malady is called polyp because it is similar to that weight represented. And of it is cancerous flesh, lapadea, of dusky color; and of it is that flesh lion not of dusky color.
Therefore, in that which is in these carns leon not fraudulent nor cancerous, you must make the patient sit between your hands and place him in the sunlight, and cover his nostrils, and pierce your finger into those carns. And then extract that to the outer parts, and then incise the water that you have comprehended of those with a sharp and subtle spatula from one side, so that you know that all the flesh is gone; if any cause of it remains, of which the abscission is possible, then you root that water with one of the subtle instruments with ease, so that nothing of it remains. But if it bleeds or an abscess occurs, then you pause to him that which you must; or if it is of the malignant abscesses, then you cut and cauterize that so that it is abscised blood and all the cavities are gone.
And then be put in the nose after the incision, either a strong wine and water, or wine. But if the nose is open and from it proceeds humidity into the palate, then you know that it is already healed.
But if the humidity does not penetrate as it should, then you know that deep within it is flesh nothing in the pus, so that the bones of the nostrils to which it has not reached abscission.
Therefore, then you must introduce a thread of lead into which there is some thickness, and use in it moth nuts, and place between each nut the quantity of the finger or less, and engage the patient and introduce one extremity of the thread into the nostril with the ray or with another cause which is possible to it.
And since that has been done, that he should smell the odor until it reaches the canals of the nostrils, and be spit on his throat, because troops of times the semblance to this action do the children in the schools, and it is lighter cause to him who wants to do it.
And then place the two extremities of the thread, one extremity that comes out over the nose and the other that remains in the nose, and then administer a lock of flesh with the nuts that are in the thread, and do this until you know that the flesh is already with the nuts of the thread.
And then extract the thread, and place in the nose after the abstraction of the blood a poultice, and do this for three days or more until the engorged corrodes all that is remaining of the flesh.
And at the end of the cause, place in the nose a lead pipe for several days until it is healed.
But if you are in need of a cure that requires, use that.
And this is the form of the instrument a capipurge maker with which are distilled oils and medicines in the nostrils:
And make a poultice of silver or lead, which is similar to a small open lamp, thus like a small bowl, and the meat similarly.
And if you wish, make a closed cannula like a pipe, and the nozzle of the instrument to be a maker of purging and be flat, uncovered; to which handle in the back of him, with which you hold when you heat in him oil or water that you wish of the suckable juices and causes.
Chapter XXV.
Of the nails in the extremities of the nose.
Many times the nail is seen in the extremities of the nose, and it is magnified and mentioned in certain days within which it is not seen the regard of it.
And for that cove that you inscribe that in the beginning of the appearance of it, and eradicate all that. And then place over the area the cautery or medicine comburent which was in the place of it.
But if its incision is late within which it is magnified, then consider if it is petrified, hard, of dusky color and of little feeling, then do not touch that with iron, for it is a dangerous abscess; for many times to him who incides this abscess, it is due to him great malice on him who had it.
And if the abscess is of leon touchment and not of dusky color, and times that incision is possible in all that, then evacuate the patient and incision that without fear, and cure the place with water that exsiccate and constrict within which it is healed.
Chapter XXVI.
Of suturing the nose and of the lips and of the auricle when the continuity is dissolved by plague or by resemblances.
Know that when accidents dissolve continuity in one of these cartilages, little confer in that operation unless in some men.
Therefore, when it is due to some cause of those, that you consider if it is fresh plague with its blood, and then you adjust the two lips of the plague with suture, and then cure that within which it is healed.
And if the lips of the solution of continuity are already separated, and each is made healthy, then you must excise each part of its code apparent until blood comes out. And then adjust the two lips with suture and strengthen those, and over those pulverize blood of drago and enses trissat, and place over the powder a piece of diafinicon plaster, or of some other of the consolidating plasters, and leave that tied for two days or three, and then untie that and change that plaster and leave that until the threads are by themselves abscinditz, and then cure with enguent until it is healed.
But the manner of the suture is that you add the solution of continuity or with needles as we have recommended in the suture of the belly, or add the solution of continuity with thread as to you and have done known.
Chapter XXVII.
Of extraction of those causes that come in the two lips in the parts of the inside.
Many times it is due to many men in the parts inside of their lips some abscesses hard resembling fat of errs, and some of them are smaller and some are larger.
Therefore, you must convert the lips and fendas each of those hardnesses from all parts, and then the abscess incision, fill the place with sagi trissat until the blood is restrained, and then be washed with vi agre and be cured the place with water that is stipticitat, until it is healed.
Chapter XXVIII.
Of incision of the flesh adjusted in the gums.
Greatly of times nay on the gum flesh adjusted which the ancients called albulus, therefore you must suspend that with onci or that you have that with the forceps and incision that you must the root, and leave the sania run or the blood.
And then place over the location sagi trissat or other stiptic or excicative powders.
And if that flesh returns, then incision that secondarily, and cauterize that, for then the cautery will not return.
Chapter XXIX.
Of reason of teeth with iron.
When on the surface of the teeth of the parts inside and of the outside and between the gums are adjusted rough, scaly, and some times are of nigras and are made citrinas or vertz until the gum sees through that corruption and are made the teeth rough.
And therefore you must make the patient sit between your hands and place the head of him in your knee, and ratz the teeth in which appear those scales and causes resembling sand until of them there remains no cause, and this do of the blackness and of the citrinitas and of the others until they are modified.
Therefore, if this is the case at the beginning of the reason, it is good; otherwise, then you repeat the reason on those the next day and the second and the third until you come to the final statement in what you want.
And know that the teeth have need of diverse forms and figures of drills according to how it is prepared for your operation. And because the razors which the tooth is scraped from the tip of the tooth are different from the razors which are scraped between the teeth according to other forms, and these forms of razors you should prepare as you will see shortly:
Chapter .xxx.
Of the Extraction of Teeth.
Take care that you cure the tooth of its pain with all care and take your time in the entire extraction of it, for there is no cause that should supplant the healing of it when it is extracted, for it is substance sublimated while it is being excused in the extraction of it.
But take care when the patient is forced to have that tooth extracted, that you firmly ascertain which tooth is the painful one, for many times the pain deceives the patient and makes him think it is in a healthy tooth, and you extract that one and then the pain does not cease until the bad and painful tooth is extracted, for we have already seen this by the operation of the barbers.
Therefore, as you must ascertain which tooth is the painful one, and that you know well, then you must scarify in the circuit of the tooth with a spatula in which there is some virtue, until it is dissolved from the tooth of the gums in every part, and then make that with your own fingers, or with subtle forceps at the beginning little by little until that concusses.
And then with a strong hold, have great firmness for good communication or strong stupor, and place the head of the patient between your own teeth that you hold well so that it does not hurt, and then pull the tooth according to rectitude in a manner that you do not break it, nor give the patient greater pain.
And therefore if it is not forgotten, then take one of those instruments, and put that one below it from every part with facility and exert the removal of it as it was done at the beginning.
But if the tooth is broken or corroded, then you must compress of cloth that break and tighten that with the extremity of a fine ray so that it is not broken in the hour of your constriction upon it with hold.
And you must work delicately with scarification in the circuit of every part of the gums and guard well your strength that you do not break that and remain a part of it, and return on the patient for the malice that may be greater than the first pain of it.
And guard yourself that you do not do what some ignorant barbers do in their fistinacio and audacity over the extraction of theirs, because they do not administer what we have recommended; for many times they bring upon men illnesses great ones that are more difficult to be extracted, and the roots of them remaugulated all, or some of those, or that you extract that with some bones of the mandible as many times as we have testified.
And after the extraction of it, the mouth should be washed with wine caut or wine agre with salt.
And if there flows blood for this, as many times it should, then sprinkle some quantity of zegi and asperge it over the wound, and if you do not confer that zegi, cauterize.
The form of the forceps and subtle hold with which the tooth is bad at the beginning be of long extremity, of short handle and thick, so that they are not bent when you must operate with them.
And be as many times of thick handle so that when you tighten with them they are not bent, be short extremities.
And they be made of Indian iron, well made of straight extremities, and in the extremities of those be teeth of which some intrude in the others and tighten by strong constriction, and some times the extremities of those be the form of the beak of a bird which has the name ciconia, so it is called, and they are of strong constriction.
Chapter .xxx.i.
Of the Extraction of Teeth and of the Extraction of the Bones of the Jaws Broken.
When you must extract a tooth which is broken and there remains some cause, then you must place upon the place a poultice with border for a day or for two until the place is softened; and then introduce some algesti or hold with the extremities of which are assembled to the beak of a bird called ciconia or guanta, the form of the forceps:
The extremities of them be made of flint as if it were a lime or rasp.
And if you do not obey to your easement with these forceps or holds, then you must cave upon the root and scrape all the flesh and introduce the instrument which has the name atali, so it is a small lever of which the form is this:
And it be of short extremity thick a little, and not be bent so that it is not broken.
Therefore, if by that the root is fore-gotten, it is good; and if not, help yourself with these other instruments of which these are the forms: the form of the first instrument is of triangular extremity in which there is some thickness:
The shape of the second instrument is that it be triangular and of a fine point.
And now you have helped with this instrument having two ramps of which this is the shape close to the following: And with other instruments which I will precede in the shape of teeth; for the scraping is done with this instrument which is assembled like a large onion, and this is the shape:
Of the triangular tip twisted or short, which is some thickness, and not embedded so that it is not twisted.
And another thing, that the instruments of the teeth move so and similarly the other instruments.
But the skilled and subtle artificer in the art, finds for himself according to what to him signifies the operation and the diverse maladies; for they are of the maladies to which the ancients did not say nor posed proper instruments, for the forms of diverse species.
But if the bone of the jaw or of the other bones of the mouth is fractured, or if you can, then it shall be cured over that in its place with what is convenient to it of the instruments and the forceps which we have said in the extraction of the roots of the teeth, and help with the gesture of which this is the shape:
And be in it some thickness little by little, and be strung or held with the mouth so that it does not escape or flee until the bone of all be set; and then restore the place with medicines convenient to that.
But if in the bone is a small puncture, then root that of that puncture and of its blackness, and cure that until it be healed.
Chapter .xxxii.
Of the cure of the teeth eminent above the others.
When the teeth are eminent not according to their natural course, for those the shape is this, and more so when it befalls women, and more so to the venerable old ladies; therefore, as you consider: if a tooth is nothing near another tooth, and it is not possible to suture or liniment it, then root that; but that it is not annexed to another tooth, then you incise that with this instrument of which this is the shape; and it is similar to a pig's snout, and be made of Indian iron of a sharp tip.
And be your incision with which you incise that in too many days because of the hardness of the tooth, and that it not be damaged by other of the teeth.
And if the eminent tooth is exposed to filing, then file that with a file of Indian iron of which this is the shape:
And be all of Indian iron the margin and well-set; and be of small points as much as a file with which needles are made, with which the tooth be filed little by little in many days with facility, so that no other tooth be damaged and broken. And then line that in the end, and root that with some razors.
And if the tooth of which one part is fractured and it lies the tongue, then as you file that until the sharpness of that fracture be stayed and leveled, and be filled so that the tongue does not touch it and the word does not break.
Chapter .xxxiii.
Of the restoration of damaged teeth with filings of gold or silver.
When it happens to the teeth in front with concussion and removal by percussion or by falling, and the patient cannot strengthen over that which he eats, and you have cured that with stiptic medicines, and it has not been of value your cure, then so that it not be broken, it is made in those that are strengthened with filings of gold and not of silver, because silver becomes green and as soon as possible, but gold no time is changed.
And be the filings in such subtlety and thickness according to the quantity that it can enter between the teeth; and the form of the straight is that you take the filing and take the double of it between two healthy teeth, and as you grow with two tortuosity of the filing between two damaged ones, or if one or many, until the thread reaches the healthy tooth of the other part. And then repeat the teyssiment into the part of which you commence, and strengthen your hand with facility and wisdom so that it not be damaged.
And be your string with which you strengthen the filings must the roots of the teeth so that it does not escape.
And then incise the extremities of the superfluous filings with the forceps or tweezers, and add those in each place with gesture, and convert those and conceal them between the healthy tooth and the damaged one, so that it does not make lesions to the tongue. And then release that as tightly as Roman.
But if it is loosened or broken, strengthen that or those with another filing in such a manner in all its time.
And this is the form of the teeth and the figure of the rectification of two healthy teeth and two damaged ones, as well as these tied:
And when it is done that a tooth or two are returned to their places after the falling of each one, and they are grown as we have recounted, and that they remain.
And that is done only by good artificers and wise men.
And sometimes it is suitable some cause of the bones of the cow, and it is made of them as the form of a tooth, and it is placed in the place in which the tooth fails, and it is strengthened as we have said, and remains and serves with that for a long time.
Chapter .xxxiv.
Of the incision of the ligament which comes in the tongue and prohibits speech.
This ligament which comes from the tongue some times or is natural with which man is born, or accidental and happens by plague which is already consolidated.
And the operation on it is that men open the mouth of the patient, his head existing in the tieu se; and elevates his tongue, and then incides that ligament nerve according to the latitude within which the tongue is freed from its recess.
But if in that there is some hardness or knotting, and this is due to the consolidation of plague, then you apply an oil and split it along the latitude within which you have incised the ligament and the knot is dissolved.
And guard yourself that no fissure is made in the flesh of the preon and that you split here the artery and blood comes.
And then wash the vestiges of the incision with water or with wine or with hot water; and then place the tongue in its place; so is the way which holds the patient the tongue in every night so that it is not consolidated another time.
But if blood flow happens, place on the location vitriol trissat; but if the blood vents, then cauterize the location with a cautery of tin which is convenient for that. And this cure with the other causes within which it is healed.
Chapter .xxxv.
Of the extraction of a ranula from the tongue.
Sometimes an abscess happens on the tongue resembling a small lump prohibiting the tongue from its natural operation; and often it is magnified within which the mouth is swollen.
And the operation on it is that men open the mouth of the patient sensing before the sun and considers the abscess.
And if you see it of a dark color or black, hard, to which the patient does not feel, then do not touch that because it is cancer.
And if it is inclined to whiteness, in which there is humidity, then you put the oil in it, and incise it with a subtle spatula, and incise it from all parts; and if blood comes to you in the hour of your operation, then you place desus zegi trissat, that is vitriol, within which the blood is restrained.
And then return to your work within which you have treated it with compliment of it, within which it is washed with wine and salt; and cure that with the other cure with water within which it is healed.
Chapter .xxxvi.
Of the extraction of an abscess of the ears and of that which is nothing in the goiter and of the other abscesses.
When in the goiter it is swollen, so are the glands which are assembled swollen, which happen outwards and are called boils, which when you cure them with water that we have said in the division and it is not healed, then you consider if the abscess is hard, of dark color, of little feeling, then do not touch that with iron.
And if it is of red color and the root is thick, then do not touch that with iron for the flow of blood; leave that within which it is matured, and then either you will extract that, or it will be broken by itself.
And if it is of white color, radon, and the root of it is subtle, then that is that it must be incised.
And the operation on it is that you consider, before your operation, if the abscess has already rested it with a complete rest or if it is diminished with any diminution; and then do make the patient stand before the sun, and his head be in the tieu se, and open the mouth of him, and the servant takes between his hands the tongue of him from below with an instrument of noble form of which this is the form:
And it is made of silver or of brass, and it is subtle with a knife.
Therefore, as you press with that the tongue and it is shown to you the abscess, and catch upon that your gaze, then take oil and put it in the opening, and pull that to the parts outwards as much as you can. Other water that you pull some cause of the tunics, and then incise that with the instrument of which this is the form:
Other water that you pull some cause of the tunics, and then incise that with the instrument of which this is the form: And it is assembled to forceps, however the extremities of it are curved, so that the orifice of each one is in the right of the other, very sharp, the which be of iron indi embedded.
But if this instrument is not to you present, then incise that with a spatula of which this is the form of it: And it is sharp on one part, not sharp on the other part.
And since you have incised a furrow, incise the other according to this same manner of incision, and then the incision the patient guard with hot water.
But if it happens flow of blood, guard with water of decoction of bark of millegrain or of leaf of myrtle and similar to that of the stiptic causes within which the flow of blood is restrained, and then cure that within which it is healed.
And sometimes there are born in the goiter other abscesses other than those boils, and incise those according to what I have said in the incision of two furrows equally.
And I have already treated a woman of an abscess which was located in the throat of her, the abscess tended to a brown color, of little feeling, the one which had already closed the throat of her, and the woman breathed with tight meat, and had already prohibited to her eating and drinking of water, and she had already arrived at death if may be for a day or for two.
And the abscess had already grown so much that it had two mouths inside which they came out through two tracts.
Therefore, I thought to treat it fistularly and I stayed in one of the openings, and I pulled out that one, and thrown out of it a large portion, and then I incised that one which I understood from the other tract, and then I opened the mouth of it, and I compressed its tongue, and then I stayed the opening in that abscess.
And then I abscised a large portion of it, and it did not run from it except a little of blood. Therefore, absolutely was the throat of that woman, and she began to drink water immediately, and then she ate food, therefore I did not cease to incise that abscess many times for a long time, and a succession of abscesses succeeded in the place of that one, which incision entered while it was prolonged.
Therefore, I brought a needle and cauterized the throat of her, therefore, as much as an addition; and then I walked around and I did not know what God had done of that after me.
Chapter .xxxvii.
Of incision of a boil when it is abscesses.
When catarr arises in the boil, and it is abscesses and it is long, then it is named perpendicular; and if it is in the part below thick and round, and then it is called a bubo.
But when it is cured with the water that we have said in the division, and it does not compare your cure, and it seems many times that abscess cauter already is out of it and it is subtle, then cover that you incised that which is aggregated round; and if it is to that length, and it is sanguine, of brown color or of black, and it is not at it feeling, then cover that you attempt the incision of it, because in that there is fear over the patient.
And when you see that according to the manner that I have said of its whiteness and length, that you make the patient to lie and press the tongue of it with an instrument of which you process its form and then stay the opening in the navel, and pull that down and incise that with one of the two instruments the ones which are said in the incision of two wells.
And cover that you do not incise that but the water that is adjusted over the natural term its addition, because if you incise of it more, you will lead its speaking.
And after the incision, use of water the one which have been recommended in the incision of two wells, and cure that while it is healing.
But if the patient was frightened by the incision of it, cover that you use of needle in the cauterization of it its fear, and the manner of the cautery in it is not but with sharp water, and it is that you stay the head of the patient in your knee, and then press the tongue of it with the instrument the one which we have said, and then take of the water the one which we have said in the chapter of cauterization, and make it with that living cause, and stay that not thick nor subtle, and of that fill the concavity of the instrument the one of which this is the form:
Be the extremity of it concavity in which is stayed the medicine as well as the concavity of a spoon of ray, and stay the instrument with the medicine over the boil, and the patient yawns over his latz so that it runs saliva or mucus of the mouth of that by the intrinsic medicine, so that it does not descend of that any cause over the throat of it and that it leads that.
And then take your hand with the medicine, and express that over the uvula for the space of half an hour until you see that being darkened and sedated by the bite of the medicine.
And if you want, take cotton and envelopes that over the extremity of the ray, and immerse the cotton in the medicine, and introduce the ray with the cotton in a tube of above until the cotton adheres in that tube, and do water as many times until you achieve water that you want of the cauterization of the uvula, and then remove that because it dried and falls about three days or four.
And if you wished that iterates the medicine, iterate that, and after the cauterization abstergeys in the circuit of the uvula with cotton embedded in border, and modifies with that water that is in the circuit of it of the medicine, and be washed with cold water, and be cured before with embrocation, and dedicate with gargles until it is healing.
And this kind of cure is more safe than incision and more long of fear.
Sometimes the uvula is cured with water that is more subtle than cauterization and incision, because it is cured with subfumigation in this manner: take calament, ysop, origua, ruda, scicados arabic, chamomile, broyda and similar to those of the herbs, and adjust all those causes, or some of them, in an olla, and be submerged with hot wine, and boil; and the olla be well washed with decent lut, and be in the middle place of the olla a tract, on which be stayed an instrument concavity of Aram according to this manner; be made of silver or of Aram.
And the extremity is inserted into the patient's mouth until the vapor reaches the uvula over the tonsil, until the uvula is somewhat obscured.
And then repeat this moistened procedure until it dries, and be careful not to do this cure at the beginning of the appearance of abscesses, because it would too much tighten the abscess, and instead of doing this, you should declare the abscess of it by cautery.
But if this instrument is not present to you, then take a cane and on the extremity of it compose a shell of wax, so that the patient's mouth be covered, because the wax shell prohibits the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this cure is good for health.
Chapter .xxxviii.
Of the extraction of thorns and of what adheres in the throat and of the other causes.
Moistened bandages adhere in the throat or a thorn of fish or any other cause.
Therefore, it is necessary that you extract what appears of those, on which you cast your gaze, because you press the tongue with an instrument before the sun, so that to you it is declared what is in the throat.
And what does not appear and is to you hidden in the throat, it is necessary that you make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, because forcibly the cause annexed the throat is thrown-up by vomit; or that the patient swallows a piece of bread or of lettuce root or a piece of dry bread; or put a piece of dry sea sponge on it, and tie it firmly with a cord, and then the patient swallows it, because when it reaches the place where the thorns are and it is extracted, then it is thrown-up. And yet take an eel and bind it with a cord, and it is placed and it is extracted; or take a pear and suck the top and suck the bottom.
But if it is not thrown-up by those causes which we have said, then use an instrument of lead according to this form:

Chapter .xxxix.
Of the extraction of bloodsucker annexed to the throat.
When the bloodsucker is medicated with what we have said in the division of the cure with medicines and does not confer, consider then in the throat of the patient to the sun, because you may have comprehended the tongue of him with the instrument which to you we have recommended.
Therefore, if you see the bloodsucker, extract that with a small pin or with a subtle gesture decently.
And if it is not to you possible to extract with that, then take a concave cannula and insert that into the throat of the patient, and then place in the concavity of the cannula a burning iron, and do this so many times (until the bloodsucker is dead and it is removed from the throat) and abstain the patient all day of water.
And then put a vessel full of warm water, and work the mouth of him in that, and wash the mouth with that; and do not swallow from that any cause and evil the water an hour after with his hand, because the bloodsucker as soon as it feels the water catches.
But if it is not thrown-up by what we have recommended, then fumigate the patient with cumin or with asafoetida by the instrument which we have recommended in the fumigation of the uvula.
Do this, so that it falls.
And there is another way of operation in fumigation of that, is that you take a bowl in which are burning coals and the bowl is covered with a cover in the middle of which is a hole, and it is composed in that hole the extremity of the instrument; and then let down the fumigation; and place the patient his mouth in the extremity of the cannula, and close his mouth so that the fumigation is not thrown-up until you know that the fumigation has reached the bloodsucker, because the bloodsucker as soon as it falls.
And if it does not catch, then you repeat the fumigation many times, and abstain the patient from drinking, and eat salty and spicy things, and do not drink water, and thus necessarily is that with this regimen it is thrown-up.
And this is the form of the instrument with which the bloodsucker of the throat is treated when it is seen; and it is similar to forceps as you see, unless that in it there is some curvature: Which is placed in the throat, and the extremity of it is similar to the beak of a stork, which is grooved, in which it is sharpened with a file, so that, when we grasp any cause, that does not let go.
Chapter XL.
In which is the sum of the discourse concerning abscesses and their incision.
Abscesses are special matters, of many kinds, according to the remembrance of them, of each one in this book.
And they are diverse in their perforation and incision in two ways, of which one is by the nature of the abscesses themselves; and by that which we comprehend of the humidities.
And the second way is according to the places in which they occur in the body, for the abscess occurring in the head is different from the abscess occurring in the buttock; and the abscess in the fleshy place is different from the abscess occurring in the joint; and to each of them is its own treatment by operation.
And more than abscesses are some which ought not to be perforated until the maturity of the matter in them or the completion of them.
There are other abscesses which ought to be treated when they are ripe, and not until the completion of them, such as abscesses that are near the joints, for when an abscess is near a joint, its being is prolonged until it can pour out that which is in the circuit of it, and then very often the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted, so that it causes contraction of that limb; or it is an abscess near a principal member, and if you delay the perforation of it until it is ripe, it will cause harm to the principal member; or it is near the buttock, therefore perforate it before it is ripe; and do not perforate it crudely, of incomplete digestion, but that which is near the buttock, so that you may not be able to reach the perineum of it and it be prolonged to the parts behind the buttock, and a fistula be made, or that it be done at the term of it that it may not be healed. But more you ought to know the hour of the perforation of the abscess, and thus it is when it is ripe.
And you ought to know that it is ripe when there is the cessation of pain and the cessation of fever, and according to the diminution of the heat and of the pulse, and the sharpness of the head of the abscess, and the other signs.
And that you ought to know that the perforation should be made in the place furthest from the abscess and of pus coming from the cure of the eminence.
And the perforation should always be made along the length of the flesh or of the body, if the abscesses are in the parts of the arms or of the feet, and in places of the tendons, of the nerves, of the arteries; and, afterwards, in all places equal in which there is no reflection; for in those which are reflected, use of perforation according to that member.
But when the abscess is in a fleshy place, then it is better that the perforation of it be made until the digestion of it is confirmed according to the completion, as we have said; for if you perforate it before that, it is prolonged the body of the pus of it and of much filth, and very often the lips of it are hardened and the depth of it.
But some abscesses are perforated according to the latitude of the flesh or of the body, as the necessity requires, or according to that which the member indigests.
And you ought to administer small perforation, and in large abscesses large perforation and many sections according to the diversity of the greatness of the abscesses.
And others are of abscesses in which the wound ought to be concaved and be incised, when it is made so that it is like bread and made at the term that it already follows, as those which occur in troops of boils and buboes.
And of those are those which are incised with section having three angles; and of those is the one in which it is incised as the figure of a myrtle leaf, as the abscesses of the angles.
And of those are those in which a round section and a lunar section, and similar to those, are administered.
But that one to whom there is no such as his abscesses are superficial, you ought to perforate that with superficial perforation, or flat only.
And you ought when the abscess is large and has already collected much matter, that you perforate it and do not take that you extract all the pus in that hour; having extracted from it one part, and then close the abscess until the next day, and then extract a larger part, and then do this many times according to the graduation until it is finished.
And especially if the patient is of feeble virtue, or pregnant woman, or little child, or old man in decrepitude, for the animal spirit many times is resolved with the egress of the matter, and very often the patient dies, and you operate on them.
But guard this chapter with great care, and in the perforation in which you perforate these abscesses, you ought to abstain from the plagues and consider therefore if the plague of the abscess is few, or is a flat section, then you administer the poultices of it or of counter-poison; and if the abscess is large, and the fissures of the perforation are many, then you ought to place a cautery in each wound until they come together.
And if in the abscess is incised a part of the wound or you have concaved it in the circuit, then you ought to complicate it with old poultice or with thread made of cloth of it, as when you have treated wounds or incisions, its humidity; and strengthen that within the third day; and then process that and cure it with that which is necessary until it is healed.
But if blood flows during the operation, then you administer cold water and wine to the which you soak a cloth, and place it over the site of the blood flow many times.
But if the bleeding persists, then you must administer treatment and care, and follow the powders that I have recommended in many places in this book and in the division.
But if it is in wintertime, and the site of the abscess is a place of many nerves, then you must wrap powders in wine and olive oil caut, and place those over the site.
And if it is in summer, and the sites are fleshy, then you must place powders soaked in water and oil or in wine and oil, and all that be cold until the third day, as we have said, and then you must untie the abscess and cleanse it, and administer in its cure what is proper to it until it is healed.
And thus is what is necessary for the knowledge of the cure of abscesses universally, but according to the way of distinction I have already said of every abscess in what manner is the way of its cure by chapters singularly.
Chapter .xl.
Of the section of abscesses that come in the neck of the head.
In the neck of the head small abscesses, and so of species of nodes that contain tunics that are to their fact; and the species of them are many.

Chapter .xlii.
Of the section of scrophulas that happen in the neck.

When the blood is cut.
