Chapter Second: Of fracture when it happens in the head.
The species of fractures are many, and their figures are diverse, and the causes are distinct, for some fractures are made by the blow of a weapon, and the blow breaks all the bones within the skull, which is left the bone, as if it were done in the shaft, and for this reason this species is called ascial; or the weapon incides some part of the bone, and incides the flesh of it and does not pass the incision into the back of it; and this species of fracture is called levitation absolute.
And the plague of these two fractures is either great or small, and of fracture that is compression or attrition; and the cause of it is a blow with a hammer or a fall on a rock, and their likenesses.
And this fracture is either penetrating that already approaches the pannicol which is left the bone; or it is in the face of the bone, and the plague of these two fractures is either great or small.
And of fracture it is that it is ammended in the bone, in the subtlety of the small hair, and for this reason this species is called capillary fracture.
And it is a fracture that is made either by a fall or by a blow of a rock and its likenesses, which makes some lamina of the bone enter into the parts within, and it is made in the concave place as if it happened to the wheels of a chariot when it happens to their blow and it is the parting of their entrance into the parts within.
And mostly it is done this in the bodies of wet bones, as if it were the heads of infants.
And to all these species of fractures, there are pains as if stars separated. And I will come with remembrance of all that in its place.
And we know all these species of fracture by detection over them and by inquiry of them with touches and examination of corrupted flesh that is over them.
And the capillary species is known by that which is discovered over the bone, and it is abstergit, and lined over it with touch, because the fracture appears black.
But in the cure of a fracture, consider at the beginning the accidents of the patient, and then if you see by the accidents that signify by manifest signification over pain, as if it were vomiting of bile, and extension, and loss of understanding, and abscess of voice, and syncope, and acute fever, and swelling of the joints and fever of those, and likenesses to them of the accidents in the major dispositions, then adopt that it is not healed.
And if you see accidents non-current, you hope for health, then you begin the cure of it.
The reason is that if the afflicted person has come to you at the beginning as he is afflicted and this is in the days of winter, then you should study the state of the bone before the fourteenth day according to all disposition.
And if it is in the days of summer, then you should study the state of the bone before the seventh day, because that which is left of the bone should be corrupted by the panniculus, and that the accidents that we have recounted may occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the panniculus covering the brain, and it is in small pieces and crushed, then you should insert the small and crushed piece, according to what I will recount to you.
And you must shave the head of the afflicted patient, and uncover the bone according to custom and manner as you are able according to the figure of the ailment, and that it be light on the patient.
And if it happens to you to detect the bone, flow of blood or abscess, then you must oppose it with what you have, and it is that you fill the place with cloths infused in wine and rose oil, and make the patient rest until the abscess is settled, and if you are sure of the flow of blood; and then begin in the circuit of the bone and in its ablation.
And this is according to one of two ways of operation.
And the one way is that you insert the bone with a straight incisor, and this is the form of it: And then administer another incisor this, a little wider than that; and this is the form of it:
And then administer the wider incisor than the second.
And you should have various numbers of incisors, of which some are wider than others; and some of them are shorter than others and are in the back of the sharpness of their extremities, and are of Indian iron or steel.
And use them with ease in percussion on the incisor so that it does not hit the head and damage it.
And if the bone is strong, hard, then you should perforate in its circuit before you administer the incisors with the drills, which are called non-deep drills.
And they are not called thus, but because those do not reach the term of the bone of the test, to that which is near it, because the drill has a round extremity or edge over that which is left the head of it sharp resembling an edge or a small circle, which prohibits being submerged and passing the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take of these drills a great number of which as many as convenience to the thickness of each bone,  so that it be to you present in all tests of the brain the extremity of which be acute, the quantity in length and brevity be according to the quantity of thickness of this test.
And this is the form of three species of drills: The quality of perforating in the circuit of the broken bone is that you pause the drill on the bone and revolve that with your fingers until you know that the bone is already penetrated; and then change the drill to another location, and pause that which is between all the tract the quantity of the thickness of the ray or around that.
And then insert with incisors that which is between each tract of the bone, and make that as subtle as you can on that in the end of lightness, as we have said, until you eradicate the bone, or with your hand, or with some other cause for some instruments which you will have prepared for that, as such as tongs and subtle forceps.
And you should guard with all caution that the incisor does not touch any cause of the ciphrac.
And therefore, as you will have circuited the bone perforating, and if it is continued with it, and you will have extracted that, you should shave and smooth that in the roughness of it of that which is in the bone with another instrument resembling an incisor, but however, it should be more thin and more subtle than the others incisors.
And if there remain any parts of the small bones and splinters, then take that with ease with that which you owe you of the instruments; and then take care of the patient with liniments and ointments which we have said.
And the other way of operation is lighter, far from pain, which says Gualia and the lauzec for great lausor; and this is the sermon of it: it is necessary to you at the beginning that you begin to discover the part of the bone of the place in which is the fracture most vehement and most manifest, until when you will have discovered that part and you will have placed below it the extremity of this incisor lent and agile of which this is the form:
And it is that the lenticular part is long and do not insert any cause, and the acute part is of it in two sides forward in length, as sometimes; that the lenticular part be adjusted to the ciphrac with the face of it incident acute in the bone.
And then strike upon the incisor of a part with a small mallet until you insert the whole bone with ease as it is revolved, and you are sure of the panniculus that no accident happens to it in any manner, and if the medicant14 were the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, and if he were sleeping.
And now if any cause annexed to the bone of the panniculus in some places of bone, insert that of it with the extremity of that lenticular incisor, and deliver that of it with ease, because it is delivered of it its nuisance and its pain.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the panniculus, and what is separated or taken from the bone is its fascia, and it remains in it roughness and subtle satagias, then when you remove that roughness and eradicate those satagias with subtle razors, which you have prepared the number, of diverse quantities so that it is possible for you to administer in every place the most convenient of them and the most right of them according to what you are doing in your operation that broken bone and its figure.
And when you administer in your ratio with the razor which you remove the bone at the beginning the most thick of those razors, and then use the most subtle of it, and do not cease to do so according to continuity until you arrive at administering the most thick and the most subtle of all of them.
But in any of the other fractures, small capillary and subtle fractures, when you administer care according to what you perceive for the rectification of it.
And it is the cause that it is not guarded over all those who have studied in that art. And this is what we have shown in great fractures.
And if the panniculus is torn when you remove the bone, then when you take a cloth according to the quantity of the wound, and submerge it in warm oil, and place it over the orifice of the wound; and then take another cloth folded or tripled and submerge it in wine and warm oil and place it over the first cloth, and do this as lightly as you can, so that you do not injure the skin; and then administer over this bandage, and do not tighten it except for the quantity that the wound has alone.
And leave this with this care for a day or two until you are sure of the abscess caut; and then untie this and administer medicines with which is superfluous dryness, such as roots of linen and flour of herbs, and powder of incense and of aristolochia, and similar to those.
And that you make of these medicines a powder that is pulverized over the wound, as they are dried; and universally, administer in this all medicine of property of which is that it abstergesca and not bite.
And guard yourself according to your virtue that the wound in your cure be clean; and that there be no in it dirt of washing or other; nor let virulence be added to it; for when it is adjusted over the panniculus of the brain, it corrupts that and you can spoil that, and it happens to the patient according to that evil malice.
And it happens in the skin of the head, as from it is modified the bone and most when the cure of it is lessened, and it is black in the surface of it; and therefore consider then if it happens to the patient the accidents that we have said, know that it is pernicious if it is all adopted.
And if it does not happen blackening but from the medicine that is placed over it, and it is in virtue of that medicine that makes that blackening, then when you take of honey one part and of warm oil three parts. And with those lineys a cloth, and then place that over the skin; and then cure this with the species of cure that convenes until it is healed.
Chapter III.
Of the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken but two parts together, or one of those sometimes, for in it you have two bones, for the part left is cartilaginous, and it is not broken and it does not happen to it but attrition and tortuosity and sinuosity.
But if one of the two parts of it is broken, then when you introduce the said little one into its nose, and that you align that fracture from within with your said finger, and with the said push of outside, until the nose returns according to its natural figure. And be done this by you with facility, and guard yourself that no pain comes to the patient from that your operation.
And if the fracture is in the most sovereign part of the nose, and the said one does not reach that, then when it is equal with the extremity of the ray in which it is small thickness.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you do the semblance of that.
And take care of it in the restoration of it in the first day of the fracture, if it is possible; and if not, then the vii or x of days the rest of the abscess caut.
And then introduce into the extremity of the nose a piece of cloth of it, if the fracture is in one part, or put two splints, if the fracture is in two parts, and be in the splint thickness of such quantity that it fills the trough of the nose.
And I say some of the ancient restorers that the splints are enclosed in butter, and are changed every day, but it is not seen to me; it ought to be that the piece is enclosed in album of cooked egg with powder of moly; and then leave the which one until the bone is set and the cartilage is hardened.
And sometimes it is introduced into the nose, in place of splint, a pain of hawk pus that is enveloped in cloth of lion; and be that already you have the fracture of the nose more vehement and that the patient is not prohibited from inspiration.
And this cause is not necessary to you; and if you want, do this, and if you want, do splints.
And if another abscess occurs after cautery, apply a poultice of cerot or coto infused in vinegar and rose oil, or diaquilon.
But if no abscess occurs after cautery, then you must apply a poultice to the outside with sesame flour and powdered incense, mixed with egg white; and place a linen cloth over it, and do not touch it with any cause.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or are crushed, then you must cut over them, and extract them with instruments that are suitable for them; and then close the incision, and treat it with water that heals and consolidates, and with suitable plasters.
And if a plague occurs in the nose, then you must treat it with poultices and administer lead pipes until it is completely healed.
Chapter IV.
Of the restoration of the jaw when it is broken.
When the jaw is broken and the fracture of it is not with a plague, then observe if the fracture is only outwardly, and is not broken in two and is curved at the parts inside, then its knowledge is lighter.
And therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, then you must introduce your left index finger into the mouth of the patient; and similarly if the fracture is on the left mandible, then introduce the index finger of your right hand; and push with that the inward flexibility of the fracture with ease to the outside parts, with your other hand from the outside of the bone; and with this decency equal them.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated in two, then you must administer extension of two parts according to rectitude until it is possible the equation of it.
And if there occurs commotion of teeth to disruption, then strengthen those of which you hope will remain with a thread of gold or silver or lead; and place a cerot on the broken mandible, and place a double cloth over it, and place over the cloth a large splint made decently, or a piece of sole equal to the length of the mandible; and tie that bandage according to how you have prepared the ligation of it and how you deem the application, until it is not destroyed.
And command the patient to use rest and tranquility, and place their food soft and lean.
And if you estimate that the figure has already been altered in any manner, then you must remove and untie that on the third day; and then rectify what has already been altered of it and apply a poultice of powdered moss, egg white, or sesame flour, because you will have removed the cerot from it, and place over the poultice a linen cloth.
And therefore, as it stands attached and adjusted that poultice over it, and the disposition of the bone is not altered, leave it, that you do not untie it until it is healed and strengthens the fracture; because many times this fracture is straightened in three weeks.
And if another time an abscess occurs after cautery, then administer what we have said many times in the sedation of it until it is healed, and the abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with a plague, then consider if it is a piece of bone separated or splinters, then you must subtilize that you remove those splinters with what you deem suitable for their removal with instruments.
And if the orifice of the plague is strait, then you must enlarge it with the fleubotomist according to the quantity of your need.
And when you will have removed those splinters, and of those nothing is removed, then you must close the orifice of the plague if it is wide; and if not, place over it one of the poultices that you deem suitable for it and heal it, until it is healed.
Chapter V.
Of the restoration of the clavicle when it is broken.
Many times the clavicle is broken at the part in front of the muscle, and that fracture is made in one of three ways.
Because it is broken, or separated, or is separated in two parts, the other which do not occur splinters; and it is easy to the restoration of it; or it is so that it occurs splinters in it, and it is difficult to the restoration of it; or it is so that the fracture is with a plague.
And the operation in that, when the fracture is with a plague, is that you make two ministers be present, so that one holds the adjutori and follows the broken clavicle, and the other extends the neck with that part.
And then engulfs the fracture with your fingers until it is made the form of it according to what you deem, and that there is in it little eminence nor concavity.
And if you deemed more extension, then you must place under the patient's shoulder a support of cloth or wool, and be the size of it according to the quantity of your need; and extend and elevate the clavicle and hold the support with your hand until you engulfs the fracture according to what you deem.
And if you cannot pull the extremity of the clavicle to the outside parts, because it is reached to the preon, then you must make the patient be supine on his neck, and place over the muscle of it a cushion of medium size.
And the minister presses the muscle of it to the lower parts while the bone of the clavicle is elevated that is in the preon in it, and then you rectify the fracture and engulfs it with your fingers.
But if you feel pangs in the collarbone and it hurts, then place your hand on that area and extract those pangs with ease.
And if it is retained in the bone, then your ingenuity makes an incision of it with one of the scissors which you have prepared for this purpose, because you have placed under the collarbone the instrument that serves the head; and it is a tool of wood or iron, of which this is the form:
And it is similar to a spoon of which this is the concavity; and its width according to what you have need of it, according to the size of the bone or the smallness of it.
But the length of it be according to what is possible to you in the operation; that it have two extremities thus as sometimes, and one extremity be wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the plaque which you have incised when you have extracted the pang from the bone is wide, and it is certain of pus caut, then you adjust the two lips of the plaque with suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear pus caut, then you fill the plaque with cloths and powders according to the quantity of the scissure of the plaque.
And if pus caut occurs, then you put a cloth in vinegar and rose oil and wine, and place it over it.
And all, that the board be not harmful over the broken bone; and for so that you see the patient every day, that when the ligament is softened or you see the board already being remauguda, then you rectify that and strengthen the ligation.
And then place the sleeping of the patient on the neck of it, and place under the little bit of it of it during the night a small cushion; so that with that it be elevated the helper of it from the side of that, and therefore is elevated the fracture of the collarbone to elevation of the muscle; or be the arm of it tied to the neck of that.
And do not untie the ligation if you do not occur in the place precisely or the pus, within twelve days.
And then renew the plaster if you have seen the face of it to that, and adjust in the stricture and restore that and leave it until it is restored and coagulated the fracture of the collarbone.
And it is strengthened and comforted according to the month in twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some men it is done in less time than that.
Chapter VI.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the shoulder.
The spatula tart is broken in the side of it and not broken of it but the edges.
And therefore when it is broken the place of it — and it is broken in the middle place of it — and it is not known except by touch, then according to what is the figure of the fracture forces you the preparation of it and reduce that according to the natural figure in every manner of engine which is to you possible.
And then place on the place flour powder with egg white and linen cloth; and then place powder of cloth duplicated; and then place over it a board made of thin board according to the quantity of the spatula whole or maybe a little.
But if under the board is concavity in some of the places of the spatula, then you fill that concavity with linen cloth, and then put the board according to equality.
And then strengthen under it with long humidity with constriction decent and firmness of the board until it be not remauguda from its place.
And consider the ligation every day; and every time that it is softened, tie another time and strengthen it and equal the board if it is remauguda from its place.
And place the patient on their side.
And the spatula is restored in twenty days or in twenty-five.
And therefore when this number is complete, then you dissolve the ligation, and that you know for sure that it is of the bones of which the beginning is not feared nor their dissolution.
And if you proceed from the place of the bone's weight and put under the shoulder, then you split over it, and take it out, and do according to what we have said in the fracture of the collarbone of the discharge of pus caut if it occurs here.
Chapter VII.
On the restoration of the fracture of the foot.
The foot sometimes is broken in the middle part of it and that is very bad; but the extremities of it must be moved that they are cicatrized and comminuted.
And of the accidents of it when the middle of it is broken it is that it declines to the parts of it and happens to it great pain, and difficulty in breathing, and cough, and strong fever and vomiting of blood; and it is declared in it cavity in the broken bone and is not hidden to the feeling.
And the restoration of it is that it be laid the patient on his back, and place between his shoulder blades a cushion; and then press on the muscles of it, and adjust the bones of it with the hands of two sides, and subtly in the operation of it and in the equation according to every manner that is to this possible and that convenes to this, until that returns the figure of the bone according to that cove.
And then place on it plaster and tow and place above the table a subtle sheet of gauze or of caligi and of their likeness in levitat, because that you envelop that in cloth; and then subtly in lian that over the broken bone that it not be harmful; and go am liadure according to roundness to the back sometimes, and strengthen that am stricture decent; and then consider the liadure in every hour, and when it is softened strengthen that.
And if necessity provokes the desliament of that, you must corrode or pruziment, the pain, the abscess that happens in the place, then you cut and untie that and eradicate the plaster and rectify that which happens by that in manner of cure of it; and then reduce the plaster, if times to that the face; and adjust to it stricture until that it be sanat.
Chapter VIII.
On the restoration of the back when it is broken.
Know that fracture of the back does not catch but in places thick that follow the back; and of the extremities of them of the part in front, it does not happen to those contricio but because that they are cartilaginous. And knowledge of it is not hidden to the feeling, therefore the inquiry of it with the said.
And the restoration is such that align the fracture with the said according to the manner possible, until that it be aligned according to that cove, and then plaster and strengthen the broken bone with steel, if it is indigenous of that.
And if the fracture of the back is declining to the parts of it, then happens to the patient great pain and puncture, as well as puncture that happens to him who has pleureci, and because that the bones put the veil; and happens to it difficulty of hanelit, and cough, and vomit of blood; and this is of difficult cure.
And already the Ancients so ingenious in that with motz engientz.
And of them it is said: cove that you put foods of the patient that engendered inflation and flatulence, because the belly be inflated and be extended, and the fracture be impelled to the parts outside.
But we would not advise that because that it not be cause leugiera to generate abscess caut, if it does not happen; and if it already happens, then it already helps to that.
And they said some of them that it be laid upon a ventouse and then that it suck with force; and it is seeming to ratiocinacio, but however it is feared that the ventouse drive the superfluities to the place by the weakness.
And they said some of them: cove that the place be covered with wool that already is submerged in hot oil, and that pulvils be placed between the ribs until that it be filled, that it be the liadure aligned when it envelops that according to roundness; and then be cured the patient with cure of pleurisy of food and of medicine.
And if cause vehement constricts the patient the which does not suffer, and the bones put the veil with puncture ledent, fear over the patient, then cove that they cut over the place, discover of the broken rib, and then place over that instrument that conserve the ciphac, the remembrance of which is preceded, and that they cut the bones with facility, and extract that.
And then that they adjust the two lips of the wound, if it is great, with sewing, and cure that with plasters until that it be healed.
And if it happens of it abscess, then you cut and put pulvils in hot oil and place over the place, and that it be cured the patient with that which cove to the abscess of it.
And be laid on his back upon the which he sleeps, until that it be healed.
Chapter IX.
On the restoration of fracture of the vertebra of the back and of the neck.
Sometimes it happens fracture to the bones of the neck, and it is very bad to their attrition, and seemingly to the vertebrae of the back. And as that happens to some and you want to know if it will heal or not, then consider, because if sometimes their hands already softened and stupefied and dead and cannot more those nor extend nor strengthen those and when you constrict those with your said or prick those with needle and no feel in those pain, then you know that it is not healed, and in the worse of dispositions it is mortal.
And if those and feel in those constriction with the said and puncture, then you know that the neck of the bone is saved and that the patient will go with cure.
And if you see in the neck vertebrae the appearance of this and want to know if it is healing or not, then consider the feet of him.
Then if you see that they are already softened, accidies to those humors that are happening in the hands, and as soon as you put your hand on their back, you feel wind and digestion, and sometimes it is raised on its back and wants to urinate and cannot, then you know that it is mortal, and therefore do not work in the cure of him.
But if you do not see any cause of those, the cause is better and less severe.
And the cure of him is that you perforate the seat of the abscess with a cautery, and you put on the affected vertebra olive oil rubbed with salt, or with mugs of hot water; put on that area several times a day until the abscess is cauterized.
And then put one of the comforting and existing plasters, and strengthen it on that with a bandage; and command the patient to use rest and tranquility and that he does not sleep on the part with which he feels pain until it is healed.
And if you see the separation of the bone from the cartilage or any cause that is already separated from it, then cover that with a bandage and that you hold that bone; and then adjust the two lips of the wound, with suture if it is large; and then cure it with some kind of ointment of the surgeons, until it is healed.
But if the tailbone is broken, and it is the end of the spine, then cover that with a bandage and that you hold that bone with the other hand according to what is possible by equation second; and then put on it a plaster and bandage, if you need those, and then strengthen that.
And if you feel cartilages broken in it, then cover that, and hold that, and cure the wound with the cure that is preceded until it is healed.
Chapter X.
Of the restoration of the  anus.
The bones of the anus are broken, and if they break, then the fracture of them is nothing but a communication in the extremities of those and they are incised in the length and decline to the parts of the intestines; and if it happens to the patient pain in the place and puncture, and the leg is stunned when it is fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand over it until it is over the fracture as is the figure of it.
And if the fracture is in the extremity of it only, then you engage that according to how it is fitted to you by equation engage until it is assembled to its natural figure.
But if the fracture is in the long one or that already declines to the parts below, then you make the patient lie on their belly until it is prepared to you the restoration of that fracture. And therefore when you have engaged it, put on it a plaster, and then put on it a bandage of cloth or of bandage, and strengthen by stricture over which you do not fear puncture of fracture nor removal of the splint.
And engage the concavity with water that fills it until that presses the stricture according to equality, and command the patient to sleep on their back or over their side.
But if you see abscess cauter, then you cease from extension of it and its restoration until the abscess is cauterized, and put on it water that sits it according to what is preceded; and then return to the restoration of it, and strengthen it thus as you cover.
And if you see in the cartilages or they are comminuted, then you put them together.
Chapter XI.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the  arm.
Arm is that which is between the shoulder and the head of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, then the restoration of it is in one of two ways, of which is the one that you take a stick of medium thickness according to this form:
And there are tied at two extremities two bandages, and then it is suspended from the elevated place.
And follow the patient on a stool; and then throw out his broken arm onto the stick until it reaches the tip of it in the middle place of which is annexed the curvature of the stick.
And then hang on it some heavy cause, or the minister extends that to the lower parts; and then the doctor and that the fracture with his hands together, until the fracture returns according to what is covered.
And the other way is that the patient be raised on their neck and suspend the arm of his collar with a bandage; and then command two ministers that one of them holds that which is above the fracture with his hands; and the other holds the pus below of it; and each of them extends to their part.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then you strengthen over the fracture with a bandage and over that with another bandage, and each of the two ministers extend to their two parts.
And therefore if the fracture is close to the extremity of the muscle, then cover that the half of the bandage is put on the citillic and the other below the fracture of the joint.
And similarly if the fracture is close to the joint, then cover that the bandage is put on that place and on that joint.
Gently guide her with ease and her work, and as soon as she is well-guided according to what is convenient and applied properly, then strengthen her if no abscess occurs after cautery.
But if an abscess occurs after cautery, then leave the stricture for seven days, and place upon it wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil until the abscess heals, and then you strengthen her.
And the way to strengthen her is that you place a plaster on the fracture, and then place a wrapping of new cloth over the plaster; and then adjust the arm on that support, and place your hand open on her muscle.
And place the cloth and strengthen over the support the arm so that the arm be in place of stars, if no prohibits this any cause of the broken bone.
And then use of stars.
And it is that you place on the fracture that star that is pus wide and pus strong of the other stars; and place between each star the width of the finger, and be the length of the stars according to the fracture with addition of three fingers of all part.
And then strengthen over the stars with the stricture which I have said at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your stricture over the place of the fracture be pus straight. And as much as it is elongated the fracture be the stricture lesser.
And therefore if you see the situation of the stars and the stricture as we have said in the hour in which you restore the limb to its situation, then you do.
And if you fear abscess caut, then you leave the stricture and the stars for seven days as we have said.
And then consider the ligament in all parts for three days so that no abscess occurs in the place proximate or inflammation; or it is prohibited the food to arrive at the limb by disposition of superfluity of stricture; and therefore rectify all that according to what we have said.
But if you are on confidence that no cause of this occurs, then do not untie the bandage until after three days or more.
And be the sleep of the patient on his back, and his hands on his stomach.
And place on the support a cushion full of wool well-guided, and consider in all hour of the night or of the day that not be undone nor dissolved the figure of the broken limb, or that be the ligament softened, therefore rectify that all with your virtue.
And place the food of the patient according to the order which we have said before, in such a way that the food be subtle in the beginning until it is begun to strengthen; and then when it thickens the food of him.
For of custom of the support and of the bed of him is that they be strengthened for 40 days; and then when they be untied and be administered bath and be medicated with poultices those which rectify that.
And if the fracture is laid rubbed, then you do not untie of that the bandage and the stars for a day or for two months.
Chapter .xii.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called two focils; of which one is small, and that is the one that sees the finger placed, and the other is great and that is the one that is placed below the little finger, from the part further back.
And strong there is broken the greater focil alone, or the little one, and strong there are broken together.
And when is broken the little focil above, then the restoration of it is lighter, and the healing of it is lighter.
And when is broken the focil of it below the fracture of it is bad, and the healing of it is difficult.
And worse of that is when the two bones are broken together.
Therefore if the bone which is broken is the little focil above, then it is convenient to the physician, must the restoration of it, that place small extension with ease until that be well-guided.
But if the great focil is that which is broken, then it is that you place extension more vehement.
And if the two focils are together broken, then it is that you place the extrication more strong.
And how you place the figure of the hand, you must the restoration of it and the extension of it extended over a cushion, and be the wrist of the hand to the parts above, more high than all the other fingers, and the little finger be lower than the other fingers, and the patient be centered on a fallen cushion.
And be a cushion in front of him in the height or straightness of him, so that the patient not incurve work.
And then the minister extends the arm from the part further back with his hand or ligament, and another minister extends above seemingly.
And then the physician guides the bones while reducing that according to the best figure that it can.
And if in the facture of the bone there are splinters, then you pierce to reduce all that in its place with your virtue.
And if appears in it splinter separated that punishes the bone and is not to your hope in the restoration of it, then you finish upon it and remove that according to the manner which we have said above.
But if the fracture is with a plaque, I have already made a singular chapter about it, and therefore take the cure of it.
And if an abscess occurs at the beginning of your restoration, then line a cloth with cerate made of rose oil and white wax, and make it between subtlety and thickness, and tighten the cloth over it with a subtle structure until the abscess is yielded; and then remove the cerate and place a prepared plaster of powdered flour mixed with egg white, and place stars; and let the star be the one placed over the fracture, pus laider and stronger.
And know that the name of the stars of the arm is seen in the greatest arrangements, or whether the fracture is in one focus or in two foci together.
And place your structure over the place of the fracture, stronger and more vehement, and as much as the constriction is above the fracture or below it, place the constriction a little more soft according to how it is preceded the recollection of it in the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths that are enveloped over the fracture be soft and moist cloths, and not hard or rough, and the thread with which it is tightened be of the proper measure between subtle and thick, as we have recounted.
And consider the limb and the joint near a few days. And if any cause occurs for which rectification is necessary, as prudently as the one that occurs to the limb, and then cover that you embroil the limb with warm water until that prudence ceases.
And let the limb not be constricted until it rests, and then return the constriction.
And if the constriction is already softened and the bone is already remade and seems to those, then rectify all that with your virtue.
And consider more if the nourishment is prohibited from reaching the limb by superfluity of constriction, for then cover that you soften it a little and let it for a few days until it runs to it the nourishment, and then tighten it.
But if the patient does not occur any cause of those that we have said, then cover that it not be untied within about 20 days or thereabouts of those.
And then let the hand of the patient be suspended by its virtue of agitated movement, and make him sleep upon his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in 30 days or 32 days, or strong it is restored in 28 days, and all that is according to the disposition of the complexities and the dispositions of the virtue.
Chapter XIII.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the thumb and fingers.
Know that to the thumb of the hand and to the knuckles of the fingers, tart end should fracture, and do not occur to them but attrition many times.
And when a fracture or attrition occurs in the hand, then cover that the patient follow straight and in front of him lies the center according to equality, and then place the hand of him over that extended.
And then the minister extends the broken bones and the doctor aligns them until they are conjoined with good conjunction.
And then cover that you place plaster and cotton, if no abscess occurs; and then place stars above according to the quantity of the place, and you have already enveloped that in cloth of it.
But if the fracture is in the parts of the fingers of the palm, and then make a semblance of a sphere of cloth, and command the patient to tighten it with his fractured palm; then it be tightened with long cloth.
And let the star be of leather with cloth in which it is elevated, so that the leather be applied to the palm; and tighten it according to what cover.
But if the fracture is in the outer parts, then cover that you place stars above and another star from the lower part in the palm, so that the hand be open and straight; and then place the constriction, as it is revolved the hand to the back between the fingers with joints.
And if a fracture occurs to one of the knuckles of the fingers, then if the finger is put, be aligned according to what cover and then be tightened with the palm and if you want, place a small straight star to it so that the fracture be elevated and not be harmed.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, as in that of the middle location, or in the index, or in the ring finger, then be aligned and tied with the finger that is next to it; or be all tied according to continuity, and it is better; or place upon it a small star, as we have said in the thumb.
And consider, in your restoration, and then that, the disposition of abscesses and oppose — with your operation — with what cover when any cause of those occurs, according to how the narration of it is iterated.
Chapter XIV.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the bone of the hip.
The bone of the hip many times is broken and is declared to the feeling according to how it is converted to the front part and to the most sovereign.
And the restoration of it is done in such a way that the ligament is tightened over the fracture and another ligament below the fracture, and the patient be prostrate upon his belly and upon his face.
And then the minister extends the whole ligament to its part according to the quality, when the fracture is in the middle part of the bone.
But if it is near the root of the thigh, then the ligament is pulled to the side of the hip, so that the extension falls upon it, and the other ligament is left at the fracture.
And similarly if the fracture is near the hip, then the ligament is pulled to the hip, and the extension is to the parts below.
And then the doctor aligns the bones with both of his hands until it is reduced according to the natural resemblance and the bone is joined by a good connection; then cover with plaster and a splint, if not, an abscess occurs in the limb. But if an abscess occurs there, then leave it for some days until the abscess has subsided, and then return to your cure.
The splint is made like this: cover the fracture with a hard bandage, and fold it two or three times, and remove the superfluity; and then wrap the leg until it reaches the knee and the root of the thigh.
And insert a long strip of cloth between the thigh and the leg from the hip to the knee, and the ends of the strip come from the side of the two parts, and then roll over the leg and thigh that which is superfluous of the bandage.
And then place on the thigh at the place of that fracture splints, and place one splint on the bone of the leg.
And then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the splint is coagulated.
And then begin with a strong splint on the middle of the fracture with three or four wrappings, and as much as you go further with the ligament from the place of the fracture, your splint should be smaller and lighter and softer.
And then extend the two ends of the strip to the parts below until it reaches the ankle of the foot, and strengthen both ends of the splints on the other side so that the ligament is not removed from its place.
And then leave the splint on it, so that it does not occur to the limb corruption or abscess, or swelling, and similar to those; and if any cause of those occurs, then cut you from it, and rectify what has occurred again, as we have said many times.
But if in the bone is a piece of the bone which puts, then cover that according to what is possible to you; and if not, cut on it and remove that, and cure with that of which is preceded the recollection until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the thigh is strengthened with other things that are adjusted to it, as we have said in the adjutant and in the arm.
But however in the restoration of it in such a manner made, do not occur to the patient lameness; and if it is restored alone, with another thing that is applied to the other leg, then it is necessary that he should limp from the one that is always.
And know that the thigh is strengthened in 40 days or a few more or a few less, and all this is according to the diversity of the complexities and of the other dispositions.
Chapter XV.

Chapter XVI.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the leg.

And if the thicker bone is broken, it is lowered below, and it is of those causes that are not to your liking.

Chapter .xx.
Of the cure of nodules that happen in the trace of any fracture.

Chapter .xxi.

When a bone fracture is restored, and then what remains the member subtle and frail, then that is not done but for many causes: of which one is multitude of solution of the ligament and of the ligation of it made not according to what is coven; or for superfluity of constriction of the ligaments until that the nourishment be prohibited to run to the member; or for multitude of superfluous embrocation; or for superfluous movement not in its hour; or for scarcity of blood in the body of the patient and for weakness of it.

And then place a poultice on the member, so that the poultice attracts to it much nourishment, or continues embrocation of it with warm water, until that the nourishment runs and returns to its natural figure.

Chapter XXIII.
Of the speech in dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of any of the joints of the body, so that it is prohibited from movement, and the figure of the limb is altered, and it brings great pains and injuries to the patient.
Therefore, when an accident occurs to any dislocation, it is necessary to immediately try to reduce it, and not delay that reduction, for if it is delayed, the place becomes inflamed and very difficult to reduce the dislocation.
And for this reason, it is not advisable to be bad or stretched during the time of inflammation, for many times it brings spasms and terrible pains.
But when this happens, it is necessary that you take care of the patient's groin, and let it rest while the inflammation subsides; and then the limb should be wrapped with warm water and oil, and then it should be reduced with ease.
And all member should be cured with that which the remembrance will come back to its place.
And I have already ordered the chapters of dislocation according to what precedes in fracture; from the part of the body more sovereign to the part less so.
Chapter XXIV.
Of the cure of dislocation of the lower jaw.
So the two jaws are dislocated if they are not treated; and the dislocation of both is done in one of two ways: that they are slightly displaced from their places and softened a little; or they are dislocated by a complete dislocation and complicated, so that they are softened at the junction where the saliva of the patient flows and that he cannot hold it or close his mouth, and his tongue is enveloped in his word.
But when the dislocation of it is slight, then in the major dispositions it returns by itself with little cause.
But if the dislocation is complete and complicated, then it is necessary that a reduction of it be administered gently, and not be delayed at all.
And it is that the minister has the head of the patient, and the doctor inserts his thumb of one hand into the root of the dislocation of the lower jaw from inside his mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or that he inserts his two thumbs together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers of his hands are outside with which he supports; and he commands the patient to cease his jaws and allow them to move from each side; and the doctor pushes the jaw and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
And if it is difficult to reduce it, and especially if both jaws are dislocated at the same time, then administer vaporization with warm water and oil, until a lighter reduction of both is made and the reduction of both is not delayed at all, as we have said.
And therefore, when they return and are set, and the mouth of the patient is closed and is not softened, then place on them powders of cloth with cerot, and it is made of wax and oil rubbed; and it is applied with ease with soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on his back, and his head be standing between two pillows, so that he does not strain either to the right or to the left; and do not let him chew for any cause; having his food be forbidden for a long time, until the pains are reduced and the jaws are healed, then let him chew what he wants.
And this should be administered with ease, and do not work on the opening of the mouth for eating and drinking and speaking, until the jaw is healed and sound.
And if it is difficult to reduce two jaws when they are dislocated in any hour and are not returned to their places, then many times this happens fever and persistent diarrhea, and very much the belly of the patient is affected, and very much and pure yellow bile is vomited.
And when you see this, then you know that it is pernicious; and many times he dies to whom this happens within ten days.

Chapter XXVI.

Chapter .xxvii.
Of the cure of dislocation of the hip.

Chapter .xxviii.
Of the cure of dislocation of the malassen, so it is of the joint that is between the arm and the hand.

And the reduction of the dislocation of that is that you place the joint of the patient's hand on a table and the minister extends the hand of that, and the doctor places his hand or his palm on the prominence of the joint, and pushes it inward until it returns to its place.
But however, it must be considered if the palm is already placed in the belly of the hand, then the patient places the back of his hand on a table, over the extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is precedent, then the back of the hand, so that the position of the hand of that intrinsically on the table, so that the doctor's hand lies on that prominence of the joint.
And therefore if it returns immediately, it is good; and if not, then strengthen that with a poultice, yielding the abscess and leaving it; and do not repeat this, for if not, do not suffer nor can you on the reduction of it more than days pass by it.
But however, the joint remains according to its tortuosity; and do not put any cause to the patient, except that the hand is softened and cannot strengthen any cause; then you know that the nerves are broken or cut, and then there is no other way than to strengthen it with a cautery near the place of it and strongly do this, conferring any cause.
Therefore, when the bad limb is returned, then place on it the poultice which have you recomposed, and let it be left and strengthened for five days; and then it is removed and left, and the hand of it is exercised.
But if the movement of it is stayed and any gravity falls on it, then gently that with water and friction several times, until it is relieved.
Chapter XXIX.
Of the cure of dislocations of the said.
Sometimes the fingers are dislocated in every part.
And when a finger is dislocated of those at the back of the palm or in the belly of it, then stretch the finger and push the dislocation of it with your thumbs, until it returns.
And then support the head of the finger, and suspend that part to which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
And then suspend that and extend it until straight it is healed in that day.
And when night comes, bind that according to that narration, and do not cease to unbind that in the day and exercise with movement, and bind that at night; and do this for several days until it is straight.
And do similarly with this, if it is dislocated in the belly of the hand, and bind that to that part and do with this your first operation, until it is healed.
And similarly do with this when it is dislocated in the other parts.
Chapter XXX.
Of the cure of dislocations of the vertebrae of the back.
When a dislocation complicated of any of the vertebrae of the back or of the neck occurs, and the movable vertebrae are removed from their places, then in that there is no cure, for death takes hold of the patient.
And a sign of this is that the digestion of the patient is outside his will, and he cannot retain that; and many times are softened some limbs of him or both legs of him or both arms of him, or one of those; or a vertebra is removed from its place, for many times it is removed and many times the removal of those in four parts is made.
Therefore, that is already removed to the parts behind and is called gelbositis.
But the cure of it is that then you consider if the gelbositis occurs from puerility, for then in that there is no cure nor from that there is healing of all.
But in that which comes from a blow or percussion or similar to those, the Ancients already said the ways of cure with verbose sermons, of which the most returns with prophecy; but I have already shortened it from that of which the little excuse of the too much of it, for my better proximity to the better understanding exposition, with which I have this exposed and formed from diverse instruments of that with which I will show this and expose that.
And of those there is no cure except that which occurs in the back properly with that which I have recomposed.
And that the patient be extended on his face, with one arm propped near a wall, and the other arm stretched out below him so that the feet of him are not lifted.
And then a straight stick be placed, stuck in the ground at the extremity of the arm near the head of it, and another stick in the opposite extremity of the arm, holding the feet of him.
And the minister holds the stick, and it is not held in a ditch; and another minister holds the other according to that form.
And then envelop over the head of the patient and below the limbs lie firmly; and extend the extremity of the limb to the stick which is opposite the head of it, and bind that in it; and then strengthen the other ligament on the hips of him the knees of him, and below the legs of him; and then adjust all the ligaments and bind those in the other stick which is opposite the feet of him.
And then the minister, having carefully extended the ligament to the place of the shaft, and not having moved the two shafts and ligaments from their places where they are placed, but however, not having stopped as we have said.
And the doctor places his palms on the spine with strength while it returns, or who places upon it a table, and then adjusts upon the table his feet while it returns.
But if it does not return in this way, then take a table of which the width or length is in the air of three spans, and carve in the wall of which we have said that it is near the patient a place in which the extremity of the table. And then place the majority of the table upon the spine, and the doctor places his feet upon the other extremity, and tightens well until the spine is shortened and returns to its place.
And if you want, open with a turn the one which is revolved with the hand; and it is that you place on the ground the head of the patient in the rear of the fist two shafts, the length of which is of an arm, and place the head of him in the rear of the fist also two shafts; and be the length that is between each shaft a palm, and already is made in each shaft a hole in which runs the turn, and each one is fixed on the ground so that it is not bad.
And be also a shaft round in which is the turn, and be revolved in the two holes of the two shafts.
And in the extremity of it is a hole in which is formed the shaft, the length of which is a palm with what is revolved; and in the two other shafts is similar to that.
And then tighten the ligaments that you have straightened in the feet of the patient in the hole which is of the head of him, and the ligaments that you have straightened in the thighs of him in the turn which is of the feet of him.
And then place each turn and stay a minister who twists the turn with his hand, and the doctor aligns the spine according to what we have said.
And as soon as the spine is returned, and the place is aligned, then cover with a plaster of drying with album of egg, and then tow; and then place upon the plaster a table of which the width is three fingers or around those, and the length of it is the quantity that embraces the place of the belly, and upon the part of the spine it is.
And then bind with the ligaments which we have said, and use the patient of subtle nourishment until he is healed.
And if there remain any prominence in the place at the end of the healing, then it is necessary that medicines be administered which soften and cleanse with administration of the table for a long time, which we have recommended; and already is administered in that place a sheet of lead.
And some times accidents happen prominence in the rear of the spines of the two, and it is cured by that dislocation; and it is that bone which already protrudes because it is not necessary that it be done proscription to it with this medicine, because very strong it will make you happen death.
Chapter .XXXI.
Of the cure of a dislocated hip.
Know that the junction of the hip and the junction of the muscle should not be only dislocation, and accidents happen to those that happen to the other junctions of small movement and concavity.
And the junction of the hip is dislocated in four ways, because it is dislocated to the front parts, or to the back parts, or to the front parts, or to the back parts.
And mostly it is dislocated to the front parts, and few to the front parts or to the back parts.
And the sign of the dislocation of it to the front parts is that, when you adjust the leg of the healthy patient with the sick one, it is longer and the bone is more prominent than of the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend his foot towards the knee, and it is the place that if the knee is positioned with a cauterized abscess, so that the head of the thigh has already arrived here.
And the sign of that one which happens dislocation is to the back parts, is that the accidents of it are contrary to this accident.
And the sign of that one which happens dislocation to the front parts is that it expands its leg according to its complement, but however it does not bend that which causes pain in the bone, and if it is forced to go and cannot go over that to the front parts; and the urine of him is retained and the groin of him is cauterized, and it is necessary to walk with him on the heel.
And the sign of that one which happens dislocation to the back parts is that it does not expand the bone, nor can it bend that which bends the groin, and the leg of him is shorter than the other, and the groin is softened, and the head of the thigh is of that place.
But the reduction of the species of this dislocation is that you consider, then, if the dislocation is old and ancient, that it is already anchored with that one, then you administer reduction of it and it remains according to its disposition, then it is not in that cure of all; and therefore it is not necessary that in that one be done proscription.
But in the case of which the dislocation is recent, and is one of the four species of dislocation, then you take hold of it so that you can turn the joint and extend it to the inside parts and the outside parts, and move it to the right and left, for it will return quickly and does not need other care.
But if it does not return in this manner, then you must prepare a strong minister and extend the leg of the patient from the back, or by his hands, or with a bandage which is tied around the leg below the knee; and another minister extends the hand of the one below so that he puts his hands below the heels of the patient; and then tighten with a bandage around the root of the thigh, and hold another minister the extremity of the bandage.
And the extension of him or from the inside part to the angle of the shoulder, or from the back part to the part of the loins.
And the extension of all those at once while the patient is elevated with his body from the ground and remains hanging.
And this species of extension is common to all four.
And therefore if the dislocation returns with what we have said, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary for all species to what I will say of the proper cure.
In the reduction of it proper, when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is in the inside parts, then the patient must be laid on his side; and then place the ligaments on the root of the thigh in that place which is between the head of the thigh and the place that is below the shoulder; and then extend the ligaments above the part of the shoulder to the highest part of the shoulder blade.
And then another minister strongly presses the arms of him, and grasps the thick part of the thigh of the patient and extends to the outside parts by vehement extension, for that returns to its place.
And this species is of the lighter cure with which this limb is reduced than the others.
But if it is excused over you and does not obey to the entrance with this species of cure of all, then it must be tied the two legs of the patient together with a strong, light bandage, on both the ankles and both the knees.
And the length of each one of its parts is the quantity of four fingers, and the leg of the patient extended more than the other by the quantity of two fingers.
And then the patient is suspended over the head of the pole, and that he is in the middle; and the length of him from the ground is the quantity of two arms.
And then command a strong young man to lessen the head of the thigh, and that the patient is suspended with another young man, and the other young man lessen the thigh with strength and impulsion, for the joint returns to its place with velocity.
But in the proper reduction, when the dislocation is in the outside part, then the patient must lie on his back as we have recounted in the having of the dislocation; and be tightened the bandage on the leg of the patient properly and on his feet.
And then two poles are placed, one to the feet of him, and the other to the head; and then poles are placed adjusted to the middle of the bed on which wet cloths are enveloped, so that the patient is not damaged because the pole is between his thighs, for that the extension to the inside parts is not pulled.
And then each minister extends his part, and the physician with his hand engulfs the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the inside parts, then the leg must be extended lightly, and it is according to this same narration on the back; and the physician places the palm of his right hand on the shoulder of the patient, and then he cuts that with the other hand, and with that cutting he pauses that extended to the inside parts to the part of the knee.
And if the dislocation is in the outside parts, then the patient must not be extended to the inside parts, and it is elevated over the ground; it must be placed on a hard cause, as well as if the dislocated part is in the outside parts, according to what we have said of the placing of it on two widths; and it is on his back and the ligaments are extended, as we have said, there is no harm.
And it must be administered compression with a table on the place where the joint is.
Therefore, as the reduction is complicated according to what you must — and the sign of the reduction of him is not hidden from you, and is that you extend the two legs of the patient, and when you see those engulfs and the patient extends his leg and expands that excuse, then you know that the limb according to what you must is returned — and then you adjust two thighs and pause plaster and extend with a bandage so that the width is not harmed in any of the parts; and adhere the patient to rest for three days or for four.
And then untie the bandage and the plaster, and compare one leg to the other.
Now, if you see those joints in alignment, then you know the dislocation is already fixed, and therefore let the patient go.
And if you see any cause for softening, then you return and plaster that, and stretch that according to your first structure, and leave it for three days.
And then unwrap that, and make that person delay walking for a while, until it is comforted.
Chapter .xxxii.
Of the cure of dislocation of the shoulder.
The shoulder is dislocated in three ways, which is dislocated to the outside parts, and to the inside parts, and to the parts in front to the back parts; and to the front parts it is not dislocated.
And the sign of dislocation of it is that you command the patient to apply their leg to the thigh; then you know that the shoulder is dislocated.
And the restoration of all the ways of it is that you make the patient straight, so that he already extends his leg, if he can above that; adhere your two to the face of it, and place his foot between your feet; and then make adhere the shoulder of it to your palms, and keep those between your fingers on the shoulder of it with strength, and another minister extends his leg while the shoulder returns to its place.
And the sign of the return of it is that his leg adheres to the thigh with light no-labor.
And then plaster that and make the leg adhere to the thigh, and then tie those together at once with a bandage for three days or for four, and then unwrap those.
And do not use the patient for walking for some days, until it is healed and comforted.
But if the reduction of it to you is excused, then administer strong extension with ligaments of which in the cure of the hernia preceding narration.
Chapter .xxxiii.
Of the cure of the dislocation of the ankle.
The ankle some times is sprained by small movement, and some times is dislocated according to compliment, and the dislocation of it is made either to the inside parts or to the outside parts.
And the sign of the dislocation of it is that you see the ankle swollen proceeding to the part to which it is dislocated.
But the cure of the sprain of it is of light reduction, and is that it be extended with ease with the hands and be aligned while it returns.
But in the cure of it when it is dislocated according to compliment, cover that you make the patient straight, and hold that one minister strong and valiant of the back part of it in the middle place of it.
And then you with your right hand the foot of the most sovereign part, and with your left hand of the part most behind the foot in the place of the heel.
And then you pull the foot to you with your right hand, and then with the left for two times, and then extend with the left; and push the foot of the foot with the right of the thigh the leg works.
And do this two times according to that we have counted.
And then push the foot of the foot to the thigh the third time, and you are at the heel.
Therefore, if it returns in one time or in two according to this manner, and if you see the foot aligned, it is good; and if not, then over it iterate the operation, because that returns.
But if to you is forbidden the reduction of it with what we have recounted, then make the patient lie on his back on the ground, and stretch a  piece of cloth on the ground fixed, and that it lies between the thighs of it, and you have already enveloped over it cloth so that it does not prick the patient.
And then the minister of it holds its thighs, and another minister extends the leg with its hands or with a ligament that lies on the neck of the foot; and then extend that each minister against extension of its part, and the cloth be extant between the thighs of the patient retaining that, so that the body of it not be pulled to the back parts of its extension.
And then the physician aligns the dislocation with its hands, and another minister holds the sound thigh to the back parts, because the dislocation returns with the ease.
And therefore, when the dislocation returns and is declared to you the health of the reduction of it, then place plaster and extend that with ligaments strongly, and tie the foot with ligaments to the thigh.
And cover that you fear or you guard yourself from the nerves which are behind on the ankle, so that the ligament over it not be vehement and damage it strongly.
And then leave that for two days or for three; and if the ligament is softened, extend that.
And then unwrap that on the third day or on the fourth, and prohibit the patient from movement for forty days. But if it is forced to go before that space, it is not done safely that the dislocation not be over it dissolved and corrupted, and then that which does not receive cure.
And if it happens to it abscess caut, then cover that you administer in the reduction of it what in the beginning of our narration, in another place of cure and of embrocation, until it is sprained.
Chapter .xxxiiii.
Of the cure of dislocation of the toes of the feet.
Have that which is dislocated be enveloped with little extension of its work; and that which is not difficult, raise it lightly.
But if the dislocation is in some joints of the heel of the foot, then have the patient place their foot on a level surface of the earth or on a table, and be standing thus as if they were walking.
And then stand near the foot, and place your foot upon that which is above the joints, and then press it with your foot with virtue until it returns, and that you see it already enveloped and does not appear in the eminence.
And then place under the foot of the patient a table that supports the whole foot, which has two heads, and then strengthen it with a decent and firm structure for three days; and then untie it and have the patient refrain from walking for many days, until it is strengthened and its return is assured.
Chapter .xxxv.
Of the species of dislocations that are made with plague or with fracture or with any of them together.
When any of those causes happen, and you engage in the cure of it and its restoration, many times death ensues.
And for this reason, you should not presume over the cure of it, but rather that one who is wise in the art, of long usage, merciful and compassionate, tardy, not bold nor presumptuous; and that uses in the beginning of medicines that cause abscesses only so much, and leaves the patient to the divine judgment, but to him whom you hope for salvation from perdition, with alleviation of the sickness, and in which you appear some hope. And therefore, strive as soon as possible to make the reduction of it at the beginning of the cause, before an abscess is formed.
And therefore, if the limb returns as we desire, then administer the regimen which causes an abscess and cures the plague with what is convenient to it of the dried herbs.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and happens in the bones shattered and separated, then you pierce the stem of them, and do in that what we have said in the simple ailments of this that is preceded in the places of those.
And guard and avoid according to your virtue that of the treatment in the ways of fear according to what has preceded me in my preaching; for this is better, you owe your Creator, and more permanent to your glory, and more greetings to your true body in your time. Thus ends the words of Albucasim perfectly from which God be blessed. Amen.
